<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Field of history focused on architecture</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Architectural history" redirects here. For the journal, see <a href="/wiki/Architectural_History_(journal)" title="Architectural History (journal)">Architectural History (journal)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article describes the history of building types and styles—<i>what</i> things were built. See <a href="/wiki/History_of_construction" title="History of construction">History of construction</a> for the history of construction tools and techniques—<i>how</i> things were built.</div>

<p>The <b>history of architecture</b> traces the changes in <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a> through various traditions, regions, overarching stylistic trends, and dates. The beginnings of all these traditions is thought to be humans satisfying the very basic need of shelter and protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The term "architecture" generally refers to buildings, but in its essence is much broader, including fields we now consider specialized forms of practice, such as <a href="/wiki/Urbanism" title="Urbanism">urbanism</a>, civil engineering, <a href="/wiki/Naval_architecture" title="Naval architecture">naval</a>, military,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Landscape_architecture" title="Landscape architecture">landscape architecture</a>.
</p><p>Trends in architecture were influenced, among other factors, by technological innovations, particularly in the 19th, 20th and 21st centuries. The improvement and/or use of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a>, tile, <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a>, and glass helped for example <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a> appear and made <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux Arts</a> more grandiose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic">Neolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Neolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_architecture" title="Neolithic architecture">Neolithic architecture</a></div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">

</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Architectural advances are an important part of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period (10,000-2000 BC), during which some of the major innovations of human history occurred. The domestication of plants and animals, for example, led to both new economics and a new relationship between people and the world, an increase in community size and permanence, a massive development of material culture and new social and ritual solutions to enable people to live together in these communities. New styles of individual structures and their combination into settlements provided the buildings required for the new lifestyle and economy, and were also an essential element of change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014148,_149_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014148,_149-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although many dwellings belonging to all prehistoric periods and also some clay models of dwellings have been uncovered enabling the creation of faithful reconstructions, they seldom included elements that may relate them to art. Some exceptions are provided by wall decorations and by finds that equally apply to Neolithic and <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> rites and art.
</p><p>In South and Southwest Asia, Neolithic <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_culture" title="Archaeological culture">cultures</a> appear soon after 10,000 BC, initially in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic_A" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic A">Pre-Pottery Neolithic A</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic_B" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic B">Pre-Pottery Neolithic B</a>) and from there spread eastwards and westwards. There are early Neolithic cultures in Southeast Anatolia, Syria and Iraq by 8000 BC, and food-producing societies first appear in southeast Europe by 7000 BC, and Central Europe by c. 5500 BC (of which the earliest cultural complexes include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C5%91r%C3%B6s_culture" title="Kőrös culture">Starčevo-Koros (Cris)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linearbandkeramic" title="Linearbandkeramic">Linearbandkeramic</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vinca_culture" title="Vinca culture">Vinča</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neolithic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_archaeological_sites" title="List of archaeological sites">settlements and "cities"</a> include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a> in Turkey, ca. 9,000 BC</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a> in the Levant, Neolithic from around 8,350 BC, arising from the earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epipaleolithic" title="Epipaleolithic">Epipaleolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian culture</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nevali_Cori" title="Nevali Cori">Nevali Cori</a> in Turkey, ca. 8,000 BC</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Çatalhöyük">Çatalhöyük</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, 7,500 BC</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, 7,000 BC</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Knap_of_Howar" title="Knap of Howar">Knap of Howar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae" title="Skara Brae">Skara Brae</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" title="Orkney Islands">Orkney Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Scotland" title="Prehistoric Scotland">Scotland</a>, from 3,500 BC</li>
<li>over 3,000 settlements of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cucuteni-Trypillian_culture" title="Cucuteni-Trypillian culture">Cucuteni-Trypillian culture</a>, some with populations up to 15,000 residents, flourished in present-day <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> from 5,400 to 2,800 BC.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesopotamian">Mesopotamian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mesopotamian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Mesopotamia" title="Architecture of Mesopotamia">Architecture of Mesopotamia</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ziggurat" title="Ziggurat">Ziggurat</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> is most noted for its construction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mud-brick" title="Mud-brick">mud-brick</a> buildings and the construction of <a href="/wiki/Ziggurat" title="Ziggurat">ziggurats</a>, occupying a prominent place in each city and consisting of an artificial mound, often rising in huge steps, surmounted by a temple. The mound was no doubt to elevate the temple to a commanding position in what was otherwise a flat river valley. The great city of <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a> had a number of religious precincts, containing many temples larger and more ambitious than any buildings previously known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The word <i>ziggurat</i> is an anglicized form of the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> word <i>ziqqurratum</i>, the name given to the solid stepped towers of mud brick. It derives from the verb <i>zaqaru</i>, ("to be high"). The buildings are described as being like mountains linking Earth and heaven. The <a href="/wiki/Ziggurat_of_Ur" title="Ziggurat of Ur">Ziggurat of Ur</a>, excavated by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Woolley" title="Leonard Woolley">Leonard Woolley</a>, is 64 by 46 meters at base and originally some 12 meters in height with three stories. It was built under <a href="/wiki/Ur-Nammu" title="Ur-Nammu">Ur-Nammu</a> (circa 2100 B.C.) and rebuilt under <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a> (555–539 B.C.), when it was increased in height to probably seven stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egyptian">Ancient Egyptian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient Egyptian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 109.33333333333px"><div style="width: 109.33333333333px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Illustrations from 1874 of <a href="/wiki/Ornament_(art)" title="Ornament (art)">ornaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pattern" title="Pattern">patterns</a> used by ancient Egyptians
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Modern imaginings of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> are heavily influenced by the surviving traces of monumental architecture. Many formal styles and motifs were established at the dawn of the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaonic</a> state, around 3100 BC. The most iconic Ancient Egyptian buildings are the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">pyramids</a>, built during the <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdoms</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>2600–1800 BC) as tombs for the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a>. However, there are also impressive temples, like the <a href="/wiki/Karnak" title="Karnak">Karnak Temple Complex</a>.
</p><p>The Ancient Egyptians believed in the <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>. They also believed that in order for their soul (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_conception_of_the_soul" title="Ancient Egyptian conception of the soul">ka</a></i>) to live eternally in their afterlife, their bodies would have to remain intact for eternity. So, they had to create a way to protect the deceased from damage and grave robbers. This way, the <a href="/wiki/Mastaba" title="Mastaba">mastaba</a> was born. These were <a href="/wiki/Adobe" title="Adobe">adobe</a> structures with flat roofs, which had underground rooms for the coffin, about 30 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">m</a> down. <a href="/wiki/Imhotep" title="Imhotep">Imhotep</a>, an ancient Egyptian priest and architect, had to design a tomb for the Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Djoser" title="Djoser">Djoser</a>. For this, he placed five mastabas, one above the next, this way creating the first Egyptian pyramid, the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Djoser" title="Pyramid of Djoser">Pyramid of Djoser</a> at <a href="/wiki/Saqqara" title="Saqqara">Saqqara</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>2667–2648 BC), which is a <a href="/wiki/Step_pyramid" title="Step pyramid">step pyramid</a>. The first smooth-sided one was built by Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Sneferu" title="Sneferu">Sneferu</a>, who ruled between <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>2613 and 2589 BC. The most imposing one is the <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Great Pyramid of Giza</a>, made for Sneferu's son: <a href="/wiki/Khufu" title="Khufu">Khufu</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>2589–2566 BC), being the last surviving <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Ancient_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Ancient World">wonder of the ancient world</a> and the largest pyramid in Egypt. The stone blocks used for pyramids were held together by <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a>, and the entire structure was covered with highly polished white limestone, with their tops topped in gold. What we see today is actually the core structure of the pyramid. Inside, narrow passages led to the royal burial chambers. Despite being highly associated with the Ancient Egypt, pyramids have been built by other civilisations to, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_civilisation" title="Maya civilisation">Mayans</a>.
</p><p>Due to the lack of resources and a shift in power towards priesthood, ancient Egyptians stepped away from pyramids, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_temple" title="Ancient Egyptian temple">temples</a> became the focal point of cult construction. Just like the pyramids, Ancient Egyptian temples were also spectacular and monumental. They evolved from small shrines made of perishable materials to large complexes, and by the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a> (circa 1550–1070 BC) they have become massive stone structures consisting of halls and courtyards. The temple represented a sort of 'cosmos' in stone, a copy of the original mound of creation on which the god could rejuvenate himself and the world. The entrance consisted of a twin gateway (<a href="/wiki/Pylon_(architecture)" title="Pylon (architecture)">pylon</a>), symbolizing the hills of the horizon. Inside there were columned halls symbolizing a primeval papyrus thicket. It was followed by a series of hallways of decreasing size, until the sanctuary was reached, where a god's cult statue was placed. Back in ancient times, temples were painted in bright colours, mainly red, blue, yellow, green, orange, and white. Because of the desert climate of Egypt, some parts of these painted surfaces were preserved well, especially in interiors.
</p><p>An architectural element specific to ancient Egyptian architecture is the <a href="/wiki/Cavetto" title="Cavetto">cavetto</a> <a href="/wiki/Cornice" title="Cornice">cornice</a> (a concave <a href="/wiki/Molding_(decorative)" title="Molding (decorative)">moulding</a>), introduced by the end of the Old Kingdom. It was widely used to accentuate the top of almost every formal pharaonic building. Because of how often it was used, it will later decorate many <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revival_architecture" title="Egyptian Revival architecture">Egyptian Revival</a> buildings and objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones201424,_25,_26_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones201424,_25,_26-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201912_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201912-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harappan">Harappan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Harappan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Harappan_architecture" title="Harappan architecture">Harappan architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px"><div style="width: 227.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 225.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Harappa_by_smn121_-20.JPG" title="The ruins of Harappa (Punjab, present–day Pakistan), circa 2550 BC"><img alt="The ruins of Harappa (Punjab, present–day Pakistan), circa 2550 BC" data-file-height="1382" data-file-width="2074" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Archaeological_Site_of_Harappa_by_smn121_-20.JPG/338px-Archaeological_Site_of_Harappa_by_smn121_-20.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Archaeological_Site_of_Harappa_by_smn121_-20.JPG/507px-Archaeological_Site_of_Harappa_by_smn121_-20.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Archaeological_Site_of_Harappa_by_smn121_-20.JPG/676px-Archaeological_Site_of_Harappa_by_smn121_-20.JPG 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>

</p>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The first Urban Civilization in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> is traceable originally to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a> mainly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohenjodaro" title="Mohenjodaro">Mohenjodaro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a>, now in modern-day Pakistan as well western states of the Republic of India. The earliest settlements are seen during the Neolithic period in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merhgarh" title="Merhgarh">Merhgarh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Pakistan)" title="Balochistan (Pakistan)">Balochistan</a>. The civilization's cities were noted for their urban planning with baked brick buildings, elaborate drainage and water systems, and handicraft (<a href="/wiki/Carnelian" title="Carnelian">carnelian</a> products, seal carving). This civilisation transitioned from the Neolithic period into the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> period and beyond with their expertise in metallurgy (copper, bronze, lead, and tin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright2009115–125_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright2009115–125-22">[22]</a></sup> Their urban centres possibly grew to contain between 30,000 and 60,000 individuals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson201829_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson201829-23">[23]</a></sup> and the civilisation itself may have contained between one and five million individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcIntosh2008187_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcIntosh2008187-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235.33333333333px"><div style="width: 235.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 233.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schema_Saeulenordnungen.jpg" title="Illustration of Doric (left three), Ionic (middle three) and Corinthian (right two) columns and entablatures"><img alt="Illustration of Doric (left three), Ionic (middle three) and Corinthian (right two) columns and entablatures" data-file-height="1608" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Schema_Saeulenordnungen.jpg/350px-Schema_Saeulenordnungen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Schema_Saeulenordnungen.jpg/526px-Schema_Saeulenordnungen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Schema_Saeulenordnungen.jpg/700px-Schema_Saeulenordnungen.jpg 2x" width="234"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Illustration of <a href="/wiki/Doric_order" title="Doric order">Doric</a> (left three), <a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic</a> (middle three) and <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian</a> (right two) <a href="/wiki/Column" title="Column">columns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entablature" title="Entablature">entablatures</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 111.33333333333px"><div style="width: 111.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 109.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Examples_of_Historical_Ornament,_Greek_by_Boston_Public_Library.jpg" title="Illustrations from 1874 of ornaments and patterns used by ancient Greeks and Romans"><img alt="Illustrations from 1874 of ornaments and patterns used by ancient Greeks and Romans" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="873" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Examples_of_Historical_Ornament%2C_Greek_by_Boston_Public_Library.jpg/164px-Examples_of_Historical_Ornament%2C_Greek_by_Boston_Public_Library.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Examples_of_Historical_Ornament%2C_Greek_by_Boston_Public_Library.jpg/246px-Examples_of_Historical_Ornament%2C_Greek_by_Boston_Public_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Examples_of_Historical_Ornament%2C_Greek_by_Boston_Public_Library.jpg/327px-Examples_of_Historical_Ornament%2C_Greek_by_Boston_Public_Library.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Illustrations from 1874 of <a href="/wiki/Ornament_(art)" title="Ornament (art)">ornaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pattern" title="Pattern">patterns</a> used by ancient Greeks and Romans
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Without a doubt, ancient Greek architecture, together with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Roman</a>, is one of the most influential styles of all time<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Source needed (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Since the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical Age</a> in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, in the 5th century BC, the Classical way of building has been deeply woven into Western understanding of architecture and, indeed, of civilization itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkins20146_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkins20146-32">[32]</a></sup> From circa circa 850 BC to circa 300 AD, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_culture" title="Ancient Greek culture">ancient Greek culture</a> flourished on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_mainland" title="Greek mainland">Greek mainland</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, and on the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> islands. Five of the <a href="/wiki/Wonders_of_the_World" title="Wonders of the World">Wonders of the World</a> were Greek: the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">Temple of Artemis</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Zeus_at_Olympia" title="Statue of Zeus at Olympia">Statue of Zeus at Olympia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_at_Halicarnassus" title="Mausoleum at Halicarnassus">Mausoleum at Halicarnassus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Colossus_of_Rhodes" title="Colossus of Rhodes">Colossus of Rhodes</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse_of_Alexandria" title="Lighthouse of Alexandria">Lighthouse of Alexandria</a>. However, Ancient Greek architecture is best known for <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_temple" title="Ancient Greek temple">its temples</a>, many of which are found throughout the region, and the <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a> is a prime example of this. Later, they will serve as inspiration for <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a> architects during the late 18th and the 19th century. The most well-known temples are the <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Erechtheion" title="Erechtheion">Erechtheion</a>, both on the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_of_Athens" title="Acropolis of Athens">Acropolis of Athens</a>. Another type of important Ancient Greek buildings were the theatres. Both temples and theatres used a complex mix of optical illusions and balanced ratios.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:118px;max-width:118px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:163px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antike_Polychromie_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1237" data-file-width="876" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Antike_Polychromie_1.jpg/116px-Antike_Polychromie_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Antike_Polychromie_1.jpg/174px-Antike_Polychromie_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Antike_Polychromie_1.jpg/232px-Antike_Polychromie_1.jpg 2x" width="116"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:220px;max-width:220px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:163px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agrigento_Concordia_Tempel_mit_Geruest.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3040" data-file-width="4048" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Agrigento_Concordia_Tempel_mit_Geruest.jpg/218px-Agrigento_Concordia_Tempel_mit_Geruest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Agrigento_Concordia_Tempel_mit_Geruest.jpg/327px-Agrigento_Concordia_Tempel_mit_Geruest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Agrigento_Concordia_Tempel_mit_Geruest.jpg/436px-Agrigento_Concordia_Tempel_mit_Geruest.jpg 2x" width="218"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Unlike how most of us see them today, all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_art" title="Ancient Egyptian art">Egyptian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_art" title="Ancient Greek art">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman</a> sculptures and temples were initially painted in bright colours. They became white because of hundreds of years of neglect and vandalism provoked by Christians during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, who saw them as 'pagan' and believed that they promoted idolatry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Ancient Greek temples usually consist of a base with continuous stairs of a few steps at each edges (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Crepidoma" title="Crepidoma">crepidoma</a></i>), a <a href="/wiki/Cella" title="Cella">cella</a> (or <i>naos</i>) with a cult statue in it, <a href="/wiki/Column" title="Column">columns</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Entablature" title="Entablature">entablature</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/Pediment" title="Pediment">pediments</a>, one on the front side and another in the back. By the 4th century BC, Greek architects and stonemasons had developed a system of rules for all buildings known as the <a href="/wiki/Classical_order" title="Classical order">orders</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Doric_order" title="Doric order">Doric</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian</a>. They are most easily recognised by their columns (especially by the <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)" title="Capital (architecture)">capitals</a>). The Doric column is stout and basic, the Ionic one is slimmer and has four <a href="/wiki/Scroll_(art)" title="Scroll (art)">scrolls</a> (called <i><a href="/wiki/Volute" title="Volute">volutes</a></i>) at the corners of the capital, and the Corinthian column is just like the Ionic one, but the capital is completely different, being decorated with <a href="/wiki/Acanthus_(ornament)" title="Acanthus (ornament)">acanthus</a> leafs and four scrolls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201914_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201914-25">[25]</a></sup> Besides columns, the frieze was different based on order. While the Doric one has <a href="/wiki/Metope" title="Metope">metopes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triglyph" title="Triglyph">triglyphs</a> with <a href="/wiki/Gutta" title="Gutta">guttae</a>, Ionic and Corinthian friezes consist of one big continuous band with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relief_(art)" title="Relief (art)">reliefs</a>.
</p><p>Besides the columns, the temples were highly decorated with sculptures, in the pediments, on the <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">friezes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metope" title="Metope">metopes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triglyph" title="Triglyph">triglyphs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ornament_(art)" title="Ornament (art)">Ornaments</a> used by Ancient Greek architects and artists include <a href="/wiki/Palmette" title="Palmette">palmettes</a>, vegetal or <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Scroll_(art)" title="Scroll (art)">scrolls</a>, lion <a href="/wiki/Mascaron_(architecture)" title="Mascaron (architecture)">mascarons</a> (mostly on lateral <a href="/wiki/Cornice" title="Cornice">cornices</a>), <a href="/wiki/Dentil" title="Dentil">dentils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acanthus_(ornament)" title="Acanthus (ornament)">acanthus</a> leafs, <a href="/wiki/Bucranium" title="Bucranium">bucrania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Festoon" title="Festoon">festoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egg-and-dart" title="Egg-and-dart">egg-and-dart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rais-de-c%C5%93ur" title="Rais-de-cœur">rais-de-cœur</a>, beads, <a href="/wiki/Meander_(art)" title="Meander (art)">meanders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acroterion" title="Acroterion">acroteria</a> at the corners of the pediments. Pretty often, ancient Greek ornaments are used continuously, as bands. They will later be used in <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_architecture" title="Etruscan architecture">Etruscan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Roman</a> and in the post-medieval styles that tried to revive Greco-Roman art and architecture, like <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a> etc.
</p><p>Looking at the archaeological remains of ancient and medieval buildings it is easy to perceive them as limestone and concrete in a grey taupe tone and make the assumption that ancient buildings were monochromatic. However, architecture was <a href="/wiki/Polychrome" title="Polychrome">polychromed</a> in much of the Ancient and Medieval world. One of the most iconic Ancient buildings, the <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 447–432 BC) in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, had details painted with vibrant reds, blues and greens. Besides ancient temples, Medieval cathedrals were never completely white. Most had colored highlights on <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)" title="Capital (architecture)">capitals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Column" title="Column">columns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This practice of coloring buildings and artworks was abandoned during the early Renaissance. This is because <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> and other Renaissance artists, including <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, promoted a color palette inspired by the ancient Greco-Roman ruins, which because of neglect and constant decay during the Middle Ages, became white despite being initially colorful. The pigments used in the ancient world were delicate and especially susceptible to weathering. Without necessary care, the colors exposed to rain, snow, dirt, and other factors, vanished over time, and this way Ancient buildings and artworks became white, like they are today and were during the Renaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman">Roman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Roman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Ancient Roman architecture</a></div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px"><div style="width: 227.33333333333px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The architecture of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a> has been one of the most influential in the world. Its legacy is evident throughout the medieval and early modern periods, and Roman buildings continue to be reused in the modern era in both <a href="/wiki/New_Classical_architecture" title="New Classical architecture">New Classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">Postmodern</a> architecture. It was particularly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_architecture" title="Ancient Greek architecture">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_architecture" title="Etruscan architecture">Etruscan</a> styles. A range of temple types was developed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_republic" title="Roman republic">republican years</a> (509–27 BC), modified from Greek and Etruscan prototypes.
</p><p>Wherever the Roman army conquered, they established towns and cities, spreading <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">their empire</a> and advancing their architectural and engineering achievements. While the most important works are to be found in Italy, Roman builders also found creative outlets in the western and eastern provinces, of which the best examples preserved are in modern-day <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>. Extravagant projects appeared, like the <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Septimius_Severus_(Leptis_Magna)" title="Arch of Septimius Severus (Leptis Magna)">Arch of Septimius Severus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Leptis_Magna" title="Leptis Magna">Leptis Magna</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, built in 216 AD), with broken pediments on all sides, or the <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Caracalla_(Thebeste)" title="Arch of Caracalla (Thebeste)">Arch of Caracalla</a> in <a href="/wiki/Theveste" title="Theveste">Thebeste</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, built in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>214 AD), with paired columns on all sides, projecting <a href="/wiki/Entablature" title="Entablature">entablatures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medallion_(architecture)" title="Medallion (architecture)">medallions</a> with divine busts. Due to the fact that the empire was formed from multiple nations and cultures, some buildings were the product of combining the Roman style with the local tradition. An example is the <a href="/wiki/Monumental_Arch_of_Palmyra" title="Monumental Arch of Palmyra">Palmyra Arch</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, built in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>212–220), <a class="external text" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Palmira._Arco_monumentale_-_DecArch_-_1-22.jpg">some of its arches being embellished with a repeated band design</a> consisting of four ovals within a circle around a <a href="/wiki/Rosette_(design)" title="Rosette (design)">rosette</a>, which are of Eastern origin.
</p><p>Surpassing most civilisations of their time<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Unreasonable claim (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, the Romans developed new engineering skills, architectural techniques and materials<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Unreasonable claim (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Among the many Roman architectural achievements were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domes" title="Domes">domes</a> (which were created for temples), baths, villas, palaces and tombs. The most well known example is the one of the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a> in Rome, being the largest surviving Roman dome and having a large <a href="/wiki/Oculus_(architecture)" title="Oculus (architecture)">oculus</a> at its centre. Another important innovation is the rounded stone arch, used in arcades, aqueducts and other structures. Besides the Greek orders (Doric, Ionic and Corinthian), the Romans invented two more. The <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_order" title="Tuscan order">Tuscan order</a> was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Doric_order" title="Doric order">Doric</a>, but with un-fluted columns and a simpler <a href="/wiki/Entablature" title="Entablature">entablature</a> with no <a href="/wiki/Triglyph" title="Triglyph">triglyphs</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guttae" title="Guttae">guttae</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Composite_order" title="Composite order">Composite</a> was a mixed <a href="/wiki/Classical_order" title="Classical order">order</a>, combining the <a href="/wiki/Volute" title="Volute">volutes</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic order</a> capital with the <a href="/wiki/Acanthus_(ornament)" title="Acanthus (ornament)">acanthus</a> leaves of the <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian order</a>.
</p><p>Between 30 and 15 BC, the architect and engineer <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Marcus Vitruvius Pollio</a> published a major treatise, <i><a href="/wiki/De_architectura" title="De architectura">De architectura</a></i>, which influenced architects around the world for centuries. As the only treatise on architecture to survive from antiquity, it has been regarded since the Renaissance as the first book on architectural theory, as well as a major source on the canon of classical architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kruft,_Hanno-Walter_1994_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kruft,_Hanno-Walter_1994-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Just like the Greeks, the Romans built <a href="/wiki/Amphitheatre" title="Amphitheatre">amphiteatres</a> too. The largest amphitheatre ever built, the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a> in Rome, could hold around 50,000 spectators. Another iconic Roman structure that demonstrates their precision and technological advancement is the <a href="/wiki/Pont_du_Gard" title="Pont du Gard">Pont du Gard</a> in southern France, the highest surviving Roman aqueduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones201446,_73,_76,_77_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones201446,_73,_76,_77-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201916_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201916-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Americas_.28Pre-Columbian.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas_(Pre-Columbian)">Americas (Pre-Columbian)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Americas (Pre-Columbian)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_architecture" title="Mesoamerican architecture">Mesoamerican architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aztec_architecture" title="Aztec architecture">Aztec architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_architecture" title="Maya architecture">Maya architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inca_architecture" title="Inca architecture">Inca architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>From over 3,000 years before the Europeans 'discovered' America, complex societies had already been established across North, Central and South America. The most complex ones were in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, notably the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_civilisation" title="Maya civilisation">Mayans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Olmecs" title="Olmecs">Olmecs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztecs</a>, but also <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Incas</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>. Although knowledge of astronomy and engineering was limited, structures and buildings were often aligned with astronomical features or with the cardinal directions. Much of the architecture developed through cultural exchange – for example the Aztecs learnt much from earlier Mayan architecture.
</p><p>Many cultures built entire cities, with monolithic temples and pyramids decoratively carved with animals, gods and kings. Most of these cities had a central plaza with governmental buildings and temples, plus public ball courts, or <i>tlachtli</i>, on raised platforms. Just like in ancient Egypt, here were built pyramids too, being generally <a href="/wiki/Step_pyramid" title="Step pyramid">stepped</a>. They were probably not used as burial chambers, but had important religious sites at the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201913_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201913-44">[44]</a></sup> They had few rooms, as interiors mattered less that the ritual presence of these imposing structures and the public ceremonies they hosted; so, platforms, altars, processional stairs, statuary, and carving were all important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After the fall of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley" title="Indus Valley">Indus Valley</a>, South Asian architecture entered the Dharmic period which saw the development of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Indian_architecture" title="Ancient Indian architecture">Ancient Indian architectural styles</a> which further developed into various unique forms in the Middle Ages, along with the combination of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Islamic_architecture" title="Indo-Islamic architecture">Islamic styles</a>, and later, other global traditions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Buddhist">Ancient Buddhist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ancient Buddhist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_architecture" title="Buddhist architecture">Buddhist architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Ruwanwelisaya" title="Ruwanwelisaya">Ruwanwelisaya</a> (<a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura" title="Anuradhapura">Anuradhapura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>), circa 140 BC (renovated early 20th century)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Buddhist architecture developed in the Indian subcontinent during the 4th and 2nd century BC, and spread first to China and then further across Asia. Three types of structures are associated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_architecture" title="Sacred architecture">religious architecture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Early_Buddhism" title="Early Buddhism">early Buddhism</a>: monasteries (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vihara" title="Vihara">viharas</a>), places to venerate relics (<a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupas</a>), and shrines or prayer halls (<a href="/wiki/Chaitya" title="Chaitya">chaityas</a>, also called <i>chaitya grihas</i>), which later came to be called temples in some places. The most iconic Buddhist type of building is the stupa, which consists of a <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">domed</a> structure containing relics, used as a place of meditation to commemorate <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>. The dome symbolised the infinite space of the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201915_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201915-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> had a significant influence on Sri Lankan architecture after its introduction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and ancient Sri Lankan architecture was mainly religious, with over 25 styles of Buddhist monasteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pieris_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pieris-50">[50]</a></sup> Monasteries were designed using the <a href="/wiki/Manjusri_Vasthu_Vidya_Sastra" title="Manjusri Vasthu Vidya Sastra">Manjusri Vasthu Vidya Sastra</a>, which outlines the layout of the structure.
</p><p>After the fall of the Gupta empire, Buddhism mainly survived in <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Palas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MHR_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-MHR-51">[51]</a></sup> and has had a significant impact on pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Bengal" title="Architecture of Bengal">Bengali architecture</a> of that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCB_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCB-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Hindu">Ancient Hindu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ancient Hindu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple_architecture" title="Hindu temple architecture">Hindu temple architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deogarh01.jpg" title="Dashavatara Temple, Deogarh (Uttar Pradesh), circa 6th century AD"><img alt="Dashavatara Temple, Deogarh (Uttar Pradesh), circa 6th century AD" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Deogarh01.jpg/300px-Deogarh01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Deogarh01.jpg/451px-Deogarh01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Deogarh01.jpg/600px-Deogarh01.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maa_Mundeshwari_Devi.jpg" title="Mundeshwari Temple (Kaimur district, Bihar), circa 7th century AD"><img alt="Mundeshwari Temple (Kaimur district, Bihar), circa 7th century AD" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Maa_Mundeshwari_Devi.jpg/300px-Maa_Mundeshwari_Devi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Maa_Mundeshwari_Devi.jpg/451px-Maa_Mundeshwari_Devi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Maa_Mundeshwari_Devi.jpg/600px-Maa_Mundeshwari_Devi.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155.33333333333px"><div style="width: 155.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 153.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1_Dancing_Shiva,_Cave_21_at_Ellora.jpg" title="Ellora Caves (Aurangabad district, Maharashtra), circa 6th century AD"><img alt="Ellora Caves (Aurangabad district, Maharashtra), circa 6th century AD" data-file-height="2247" data-file-width="2298" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1_Dancing_Shiva%2C_Cave_21_at_Ellora.jpg/230px-1_Dancing_Shiva%2C_Cave_21_at_Ellora.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1_Dancing_Shiva%2C_Cave_21_at_Ellora.jpg/346px-1_Dancing_Shiva%2C_Cave_21_at_Ellora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1_Dancing_Shiva%2C_Cave_21_at_Ellora.jpg/460px-1_Dancing_Shiva%2C_Cave_21_at_Ellora.jpg 2x" width="154"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Ellora_Caves" title="Ellora Caves">Ellora Caves</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aurangabad_district,_Maharashtra" title="Aurangabad district, Maharashtra">Aurangabad district, Maharashtra</a>), circa 6th century AD
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Across the Indian subcontinent, Hindu architecture evolved from simple <a href="/wiki/Indian_rock-cut_architecture" title="Indian rock-cut architecture">rock-cut cave shrines</a> to monumental temples. From the 4th to 5th centuries AD, Hindu temples were adapted to the worship of different deities and regional beliefs, and by the 6th or 7th centuries larger examples had evolved into towering brick or stone-built structures that symbolise the sacred five-peaked <a href="/wiki/Mount_Meru" title="Mount Meru">Mount Meru</a>. Influenced by early <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_architecture" title="Buddhist architecture">Buddhist</a> <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupas</a>, the architecture was not designed for collective worship, but had areas for worshippers to leave offerings and perform rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201919_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201919-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Indian architectural styles for structures such as temples, statues, homes, markets, gardens and planning are as described in <a href="/wiki/Hindu_texts" title="Hindu texts">Hindu texts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAcharya1927xviii-xx_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAcharya1927xviii-xx-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sinha_1998_pp._27–41_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sinha_1998_pp._27–41-55">[55]</a></sup> The architectural guidelines survive in Sanskrit manuscripts and in some cases also in other regional languages. These include the <a href="/wiki/Vastu_shastra" title="Vastu shastra">Vastu shastras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shilpa_Shastras" title="Shilpa Shastras">Shilpa Shastras</a>, the <i>Brihat Samhita</i>, architectural portions of the Puranas and the Agamas, and regional texts such as the <a href="/wiki/Manasara" title="Manasara">Manasara</a> among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAcharya1927xviii-xx,_Appendix_I_lists_hundreds_of_Hindu_architectural_texts_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAcharya1927xviii-xx,_Appendix_I_lists_hundreds_of_Hindu_architectural_texts-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShukla1993_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShukla1993-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since this architectural style emerged in the classical period, it has had a considerable influence on various medieval architectural styles like that of the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ru-Gurjara_architecture" title="Māru-Gurjara architecture">Gurjaras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_architecture" title="Dravidian architecture">Dravidians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemadpanti_architecture" title="Hemadpanti architecture">Deccan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_architecture" title="Kalinga architecture">Odias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Bengal" title="Architecture of Bengal">Bengalis</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nilachal_architecture" title="Nilachal architecture">Assamese</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maru_Gurjara">Maru Gurjara</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Maru Gurjara">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ru-Gurjara_architecture" title="Māru-Gurjara architecture">Māru-Gurjara architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Gujarat" title="Architecture of Gujarat">Architecture of Gujarat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Rajasthan" title="Architecture of Rajasthan">Architecture of Rajasthan</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 228px"><div style="width: 228px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 203.33333333333px"><div style="width: 203.33333333333px">

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 157.33333333333px"><div style="width: 157.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 155.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delwada.jpg" title="Interior of the Jain Vimal Vasahi Temple (Mount Abu), 1031[58]"><img alt="Interior of the Jain Vimal Vasahi Temple (Mount Abu), 1031[58]" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="559" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Delwada.jpg/233px-Delwada.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Delwada.jpg/349px-Delwada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Delwada.jpg/465px-Delwada.jpg 2x" width="156"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>This style of North Indian architecture has been observed in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain</a> places of worship and congregation. It emerged in the 11th to 13th centuries under the <a href="/wiki/Chaulukya_dynasty" title="Chaulukya dynasty">Chaulukya</a> (Solanki) period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> It eventually became more popular among the Jain communities who spread it in the greater region and across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> These structures have the unique features like a large number of projections on external walls with sharply carved statues, and several <a href="/wiki/Urushringa" title="Urushringa">urushringa</a> spirelets on the main <a href="/wiki/Shikhara" title="Shikhara">shikhara</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Himalayan">Himalayan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Himalayan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Nepal" title="Architecture of Nepal">Architecture of Nepal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Tibet" title="Architecture of Tibet">Architecture of Tibet</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The Himalayas are inhabited by various people groups including the <a href="/wiki/Pahari_people_(Nepal)" title="Pahari people (Nepal)">Paharis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kashmiri_people" title="Kashmiri people">Kashmiris</a>, and many more groups. Being from different religious and ethnic backgrounds, the architecture has also had multiple influences. Considering the logistical difficulties and slower pace of life in the Himalayas, artisans have that the time to make intricate wood carvings and paintings accompanied by ornamental metal work and stone sculptures that are reflected in religious as well as civic and military buildings. These styles exist in different forms from <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> to <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nagaland" title="Nagaland">Nagaland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> A common feature is observed in the slanted layered roofs on temples, mosques, and civic buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dravidian">Dravidian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Dravidian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_architecture" title="Dravidian architecture">Dravidian architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 228.66666666667px"><div style="width: 228.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 226.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SaluvanakuppamVel.jpg" title="Stone vel on a brick platform at the entrance to the Murugan Temple, Saluvankuppam, 300 BC[63][64]"><img alt="Stone vel on a brick platform at the entrance to the Murugan Temple, Saluvankuppam, 300 BC[63][64]" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SaluvanakuppamVel.jpg/340px-SaluvanakuppamVel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SaluvanakuppamVel.jpg/511px-SaluvanakuppamVel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SaluvanakuppamVel.jpg/680px-SaluvanakuppamVel.jpg 2x" width="227"/></a></div></div>

<p>Vitthala Temple (<a href="/wiki/Hampi" title="Hampi">Hampi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>), circa 16th century
</p>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Tamil_Nadu" title="Architecture of Tamil Nadu">Architecture of Tamil Nadu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Kerala" title="Architecture of Kerala">Architecture of Kerala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Karnataka" title="Architecture of Karnataka">Architecture of Karnataka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pallava_art_and_architecture" title="Pallava art and architecture">Pallava art and architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pandyan_art_and_architecture" title="Pandyan art and architecture">Pandyan art and architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chola_art_and_architecture" title="Chola art and architecture">Chola art and architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kadamba_architecture" title="Kadamba architecture">Kadamba architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Badami_Chalukya_architecture" title="Badami Chalukya architecture">Badami Chalukya architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hoysala_architecture" title="Hoysala architecture">Hoysala architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vijayanagara_architecture" title="Vijayanagara architecture">Vijayanagara architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Western_Chalukya_architecture" title="Western Chalukya architecture">Western Chalukya architecture</a></div>
<p>This is an architectural style that emerged in the southern part of the Indian subcontinent and in Sri Lanka. These include <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temples</a> with a unique style that involves a shorter pyramidal tower over the <a href="/wiki/Garbhagriha" title="Garbhagriha">garbhagriha</a> or sanctuary called a <a href="/wiki/Vimana_(architectural_feature)" title="Vimana (architectural feature)">vimana</a>, where the north has taller towers, usually bending inwards as they rise, called <a href="/wiki/Shikhara" title="Shikhara">shikharas</a>. These also include secular buildings that may or may not have slanted roofs based on the geographical region. In the Tamil country, this style is influenced by the Sangam period as well as the styles of the great dynasties that ruled it. This style varies in the region to its west in Kerala that is influenced by geographic factors like western trade and the monsoons which result in sloped roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Further north, the <a href="/wiki/Vesara" title="Vesara">Karnata Dravida</a> style varies based on the diversity of influences, often relaying much about the artistic trends of the rulers of twelve different dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kalinga">Kalinga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Kalinga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_architecture" title="Kalinga architecture">Kalinga architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 223.33333333333px"><div style="width: 223.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 221.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temple-Jagannath.jpg" title="The Jagannath Temple (Puri, Odisha, India), one of the four holiest places (Dhamas) of Hinduism,[67] 12th century"><img alt="The Jagannath Temple (Puri, Odisha, India), one of the four holiest places (Dhamas) of Hinduism,[67] 12th century" data-file-height="543" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Temple-Jagannath.jpg/332px-Temple-Jagannath.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Temple-Jagannath.jpg/498px-Temple-Jagannath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Temple-Jagannath.jpg/663px-Temple-Jagannath.jpg 2x" width="222"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Jagannath_Temple,_Puri#Structure" title="Jagannath Temple, Puri">Jagannath Temple</a> (<a href="/wiki/Puri" title="Puri">Puri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, India), one of the four holiest places (Dhamas) of Hinduism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> 12th century
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px"><div style="width: 227.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 225.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Konarka_Temple.jpg" title="The Konark Sun Temple (Puri), circa 1250"><img alt="The Konark Sun Temple (Puri), circa 1250" data-file-height="1366" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Konarka_Temple.jpg/338px-Konarka_Temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Konarka_Temple.jpg/507px-Konarka_Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Konarka_Temple.jpg/675px-Konarka_Temple.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simplified_schema_of_Kalinga_architecture.jpg" title="Simplified schema of a Kalinga temple"><img alt="Simplified schema of a Kalinga temple" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Simplified_schema_of_Kalinga_architecture.jpg/300px-Simplified_schema_of_Kalinga_architecture.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Simplified_schema_of_Kalinga_architecture.jpg/451px-Simplified_schema_of_Kalinga_architecture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Simplified_schema_of_Kalinga_architecture.jpg/600px-Simplified_schema_of_Kalinga_architecture.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Simplified schema of a Kalinga temple
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The ancient Kalinga region corresponds to the present-day eastern Indian areas of <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a>. Its architecture reached a peak between the 9th and 12th centuries under the patronage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somavamsi_dynasty" title="Somavamsi dynasty">Somavamsi dynasty</a> of Odisha. Lavishly sculpted with hundreds of figures, Kalinga temples usually feature repeating forms such as horseshoes. Within the protective walls of the temple complex are three main buildings with distinctive curved towers called <i>deul</i> or <i>deula</i> and prayer halls called <i>jagmohan</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201921_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201921-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="East_and_Southeast_Asia">East and Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: East and Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 176px"><div style="width: 176px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px"><div style="width: 227.33333333333px">

<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kinkaku-ji_the_Golden_Temple_in_Kyoto_overlooking_the_lake_-_high_rez.JPG" title="Kinkaku-ji (Kitayama, Kyoto), a Zen Buddhist temple in Kyoto, 1398"><img alt="Kinkaku-ji (Kitayama, Kyoto), a Zen Buddhist temple in Kyoto, 1398" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Kinkaku-ji_the_Golden_Temple_in_Kyoto_overlooking_the_lake_-_high_rez.JPG/300px-Kinkaku-ji_the_Golden_Temple_in_Kyoto_overlooking_the_lake_-_high_rez.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Kinkaku-ji_the_Golden_Temple_in_Kyoto_overlooking_the_lake_-_high_rez.JPG/451px-Kinkaku-ji_the_Golden_Temple_in_Kyoto_overlooking_the_lake_-_high_rez.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Kinkaku-ji_the_Golden_Temple_in_Kyoto_overlooking_the_lake_-_high_rez.JPG/600px-Kinkaku-ji_the_Golden_Temple_in_Kyoto_overlooking_the_lake_-_high_rez.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Kinkaku-ji" title="Kinkaku-ji">Kinkaku-ji</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kitayama" title="Kitayama">Kitayama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a> <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_temples_in_Japan" title="Buddhist temples in Japan">Buddhist temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, 1398
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_garden" title="Chinese garden">Chinese garden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_architecture" title="Korean architecture">Korean architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a></div>
<p>What is recognised today as Chinese culture has its roots in the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period (10,000–2000 BC), covering the cultural sites of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangshao" title="Yangshao">Yangshao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Longshan_culture" title="Longshan culture">Longshan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liangzhu_culture" title="Liangzhu culture">Liangzhu</a> in central China.  Sections of present-day north-east China also contain sites of the Neolithic <a href="/wiki/Hongshan_culture" title="Hongshan culture">Hongshan</a> culture that manifested aspects of proto-Chinese culture. Native Chinese belief systems included naturalistic, <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> and hero worship. In general, open-air platforms (<i>tan</i>, or altar) were used for worshipping naturalistic deities, such as the gods of wind and earth, whereas formal buildings (<i>miao</i>, or temple) were for heroes and deceased ancestors.
</p><p>Most early buildings in China were <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">timber</a> structures. Columns with sets of <a href="/wiki/Bracket_(architecture)" title="Bracket (architecture)">brackets</a> on the face of the buildings, mostly in even numbers, made the central intercolumnal space the largest interior opening. Heavily tiled roofs sat squarely on the timber building with walls constructed in brick or pounded earth.
</p><p>The transmission of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> into China around the 1st century AD led to a new era of religious practices, and so to new building types. Places of worship in form of cave temples appeared in China, based on <a href="/wiki/Indian_rock-cut_architecture" title="Indian rock-cut architecture">Indian rock-cut</a> ones. Another new building type introduced by Buddhism was the Chinese form of <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a> (<i>ta</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagoda</a>. In India, stupas were erected to commemorate well-known people or teachers: consequently, the Buddhist tradition adapted the structure to remember the great teacher, the Buddha. In The Chinese pagoda shared a similar symbolism with the Indian stupa and was built with sponsorship mainly from imperial patrons who hoped to gain earthly merits for the next life. Buddhism reached its peak from the 6th to the 8th centuries when there was an unprecedented number of monasteries thought China. More than 4,600 official and 40,000 unofficial monasteries were built. They varies in size by the number of cloisters they conatined, ranging from 6 to 120. Each cloister consisted of a main stand-alone building – a hall, pagoda of pavilion – and was surrounded by a covered corridor in a rectangular compounded served by a gate building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones201454,_55,_56_&amp;_57_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones201454,_55,_56_&amp;_57-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian</a> culture has had a significant influence on the art and architecture in the <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">Sinosphere</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Korean_architecture" title="Korean architecture">Korean architecture</a>, especially post <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Choson</a> period showcases <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a>-<a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing</a> influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, Japanese architecture was made of wood and <i><a href="/wiki/Fusuma" title="Fusuma">fusuma</a></i> (sliding doors) in place of walls, allowing internal space to be altered to suit different purposes. The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in the mid 6th century, via the neighbouring Korean kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paekche" title="Paekche">Paekche</a>, initiated large-scale wooden temple building with an emphasis on simplicity, and much of the architecture was imported from China and other Asian cultures. By the end of this century, Japan was constructing Continental-style monasteries, notably the temple, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horyu-ji" title="Horyu-ji">Horyu-ji</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ikaruga" title="Ikaruga">Ikaruga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones201463_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones201463-72">[72]</a></sup> In contrast with Western architecture, Japanese structures rarely use stone, except for specific elements such as foundations. Walls are light, thin, never load-bearing and often movable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kruft,_Hanno-Walter_1994_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kruft,_Hanno-Walter_1994-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Khmer">Khmer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Khmer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Khmer_architecture" title="Khmer architecture">Khmer architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
