<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/M%E1%BB%B9_S%C6%A1n" title="Mỹ Sơn">Champa Temples</a> (<a href="/wiki/M%E1%BB%B9_S%C6%A1n" title="Mỹ Sơn">Mỹ Sơn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>), circa 4th century AD
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>From the start of the 9th century to the early 15th century, Khmer kings rules over a vad Hindu-Buddhist empire in Southeast Asia. <a href="/wiki/Angkor" title="Angkor">Angkor</a>, in present-day <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, was its capital city, and most of its surviving buildings are east-facing stone temples, many of them constructed in pyramidal, tiered form consisting of five square structures with towers, or <i>prasats</i>, that represent the sacred five-peaked <a href="/wiki/Mount_Meru" title="Mount Meru">Mount Meru</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> doctrine. As the residences of gods, temples were made of durable materials such as <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a>, brick or <a href="/wiki/Laterite" title="Laterite">laterite</a>, a clay-like substance that dries hard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201923_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201923-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Cham</a> architecture in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> also follows a similar style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-so04.tci-thaijo.org_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-so04.tci-thaijo.org-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sub-Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Africa" title="Architecture of Africa">Architecture of Africa</a></div>
<p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan African</a> architecture is diverse, varying significantly across regions. Included among traditional house types, are huts, sometimes consisting of one or two rooms, as well as various larger and more complex structures.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="West_African_and_Bantu_styles">West African and Bantu styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: West African and Bantu styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 108px"><div style="width: 108px">

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 176px"><div style="width: 176px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 174px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anna_Hinderer_building.jpg" title="Yoruba architecture depicted in a book by Anna Hinderer in the mid-1800s[75]"><img alt="Yoruba architecture depicted in a book by Anna Hinderer in the mid-1800s[75]" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="423" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Anna_Hinderer_building.jpg/261px-Anna_Hinderer_building.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Anna_Hinderer_building.jpg/392px-Anna_Hinderer_building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Anna_Hinderer_building.jpg 2x" width="174"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Yoruba_architecture" title="Yoruba architecture">Yoruba architecture</a> depicted in a book by <a href="/wiki/Anna_Hinderer" title="Anna Hinderer">Anna Hinderer</a> in the mid-1800s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:07_-_(Die_sheng)_le_symbole_supreme_et_representatif_de_la_chefferie_et_du_peuple_Bandjoun.JPG" title="Traditional Bamileke architecture, main hall of the Bandjoun palace in Cameroon, with decoratively carved wooden columns and doorway, unknown date"><img alt="Traditional Bamileke architecture, main hall of the Bandjoun palace in Cameroon, with decoratively carved wooden columns and doorway, unknown date" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/07_-_%28Die_sheng%29_le_symbole_supreme_et_representatif_de_la_chefferie_et_du_peuple_Bandjoun.JPG/300px-07_-_%28Die_sheng%29_le_symbole_supreme_et_representatif_de_la_chefferie_et_du_peuple_Bandjoun.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/07_-_%28Die_sheng%29_le_symbole_supreme_et_representatif_de_la_chefferie_et_du_peuple_Bandjoun.JPG/451px-07_-_%28Die_sheng%29_le_symbole_supreme_et_representatif_de_la_chefferie_et_du_peuple_Bandjoun.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/07_-_%28Die_sheng%29_le_symbole_supreme_et_representatif_de_la_chefferie_et_du_peuple_Bandjoun.JPG/600px-07_-_%28Die_sheng%29_le_symbole_supreme_et_representatif_de_la_chefferie_et_du_peuple_Bandjoun.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Bamileke_people" title="Bamileke people">Bamileke</a> architecture, main hall of the <a href="/wiki/Bandjoun" title="Bandjoun">Bandjoun</a> palace in Cameroon, with decoratively carved wooden columns and doorway, unknown date
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In much of West Africa, rectangular houses with peaked roofs and courtyards, sometimes consisting of several rooms and courtyards, are also traditionally found (sometimes decorated, with adobe reliefs as among the <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_Empire" title="Ashanti Empire">Ashanti</a> of Ghana,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> or carved pillars as among the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba people</a> of Nigeria, especially in palaces and the dwellings of the wealthy)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Besides the regular rectangular type of dwelling with a sharp roof, widespread in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, there also other types of houses: <a href="/wiki/Beehive_house" title="Beehive house">beehive houses</a> made from a circle of stones topped with a domed roof, and the round one, with a <a href="/wiki/Cone" title="Cone">cone</a>-shaped roof. The first type, which also existed in America, is characteristic especially for Southern Africa. These were used by <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a>-speaking groups in southern and parts of east Africa, which was made with mud, poles, thatch, and <a href="/wiki/Cow_dung" title="Cow dung">cow dung</a> (rectangular houses were more common among the Bantu-speaking peoples of the greater Congo region and central Africa). The round hut with a <a href="/wiki/Cone" title="Cone">cone</a>-shaped roof is widespread especially in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Africa" title="Eastern Africa">Eastern Africa</a>, but is also present in <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, as well as in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Sudan" title="Western Sudan">Western Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> regions of west Africa, where they are sometimes arranged into compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A distinct style of traditional wooden architecture exists among the Grassland peoples of Cameroon, such as the <a href="/wiki/Bamileke_people" title="Bamileke people">Bamileke</a>.
</p><p>In several West African societies, including the kingdom of Benin (and of other <a href="/wiki/Edo_people" title="Edo people">Edo peoples</a>), and the kingdoms of the Yoruba, Hausa, at sites like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jenne-Jeno" title="Jenne-Jeno">Jenne-Jeno</a> (a pre-Islamic city in Mali),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAC_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAC-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and elsewhere, towns and cities were surrounded by large walls of mud brick or adobe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and sometimes by monumental moats and earthworks, such as <a href="/wiki/Sungbo%27s_Eredo" title="Sungbo's Eredo">Sungbo's Eredo</a> (in the Nigerian Yoruba kingdom of Ijebu) and the <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Benin" title="Walls of Benin">Walls of Benin</a> (of the Nigerian <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Benin" title="Kingdom of Benin">Kingdom of Benin</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protection_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Protection-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-84">[84]</a></sup> In medieval southern Africa, a tradition existed of fortified stone settlements such as <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khami" title="Khami">Khami</a>.
</p><p>The famed <a href="/wiki/Benin_City" title="Benin City">Benin City</a> of southwest Nigeria (capital of the Kingdom of Benin) destroyed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punitive_Expedition" title="Punitive Expedition">Punitive Expedition</a>, was a large complex of homes in coursed clay, with <a href="/wiki/Hip_roof" title="Hip roof">hipped roofs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Roof_shingle" title="Roof shingle">shingles</a> or palm leaves. The Palace had a sequence of ceremonial rooms, and was decorated with <a href="/wiki/Benin_Bronzes" title="Benin Bronzes">brass plaques</a>. It was surrounded by a monumental complex of <a href="/wiki/Walls_of_Benin" title="Walls of Benin">earthworks and walls</a> whose construction is thought to have began by the early Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protection_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Protection-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ogundiran_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ogundiran-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacEachern_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacEachern-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px"><div style="width: 227.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 225.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cam0492_Habitation_de_Pouss.jpg" title="Beehive-shaped houses of the Musgum ethnic group in Pouss (Cameroon), unknown date"><img alt="Beehive-shaped houses of the Musgum ethnic group in Pouss (Cameroon), unknown date" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Cam0492_Habitation_de_Pouss.jpg/338px-Cam0492_Habitation_de_Pouss.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Cam0492_Habitation_de_Pouss.jpg/507px-Cam0492_Habitation_de_Pouss.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Cam0492_Habitation_de_Pouss.jpg/675px-Cam0492_Habitation_de_Pouss.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 239.33333333333px"><div style="width: 239.33333333333px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sahelian">Sahelian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sahelian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sudano-Sahelian_architecture" title="Sudano-Sahelian architecture">Sudano-Sahelian architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 228px"><div style="width: 228px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 226px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg" title="The Great Mosque of Djenné (Djenné, Mali), an icon for the Sudano-Sahelian architecture, originally built in the 13th-14th centuries, rebuilt in 1907, adobe"><img alt="The Great Mosque of Djenné (Djenné, Mali), an icon for the Sudano-Sahelian architecture, originally built in the 13th-14th centuries, rebuilt in 1907, adobe" data-file-height="1362" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg/339px-Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg/508px-Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg/677px-Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Djenn%C3%A9" title="Great Mosque of Djenné">Great Mosque of Djenné</a> (<a href="/wiki/Djenn%C3%A9" title="Djenné">Djenné</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>), an icon for the <a href="/wiki/Sudano-Sahelian_architecture" title="Sudano-Sahelian architecture">Sudano-Sahelian architecture</a>, originally built in the 13th-14th centuries, rebuilt in 1907, <a href="/wiki/Adobe" title="Adobe">adobe</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 228px"><div style="width: 228px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 226px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Larabanga_Mosque_Ghana.jpg" title="The Larabanga Mosque (Larabanga, northern Ghana), possibly built in the 15th century"><img alt="The Larabanga Mosque (Larabanga, northern Ghana), possibly built in the 15th century" data-file-height="1457" data-file-width="2194" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Larabanga_Mosque_Ghana.jpg/339px-Larabanga_Mosque_Ghana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Larabanga_Mosque_Ghana.jpg/509px-Larabanga_Mosque_Ghana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Larabanga_Mosque_Ghana.jpg/678px-Larabanga_Mosque_Ghana.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Larabanga_Mosque" title="Larabanga Mosque">Larabanga Mosque</a> (<a href="/wiki/Larabanga" title="Larabanga">Larabanga</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>), possibly built in the 15th century
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 114px"><div style="width: 114px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 112px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kanogate.jpg" title="Hausa gate, the Gidan Rumfa in Kano, northern Nigeria, 15th century"><img alt="Hausa gate, the Gidan Rumfa in Kano, northern Nigeria, 15th century" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="1712" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Kanogate.jpg/168px-Kanogate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Kanogate.jpg/253px-Kanogate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Kanogate.jpg/337px-Kanogate.jpg 2x" width="112"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a> gate, the <a href="/wiki/Gidan_Rumfa" title="Gidan Rumfa">Gidan Rumfa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kano_(city)" title="Kano (city)">Kano</a>, northern Nigeria, 15th century
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In the Western <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> region, Islamic influence was a major contributing factor to architectural development from the later ages of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ghana" title="Kingdom of Ghana">Kingdom of Ghana</a>. At <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kumbi_Saleh" title="Kumbi Saleh">Kumbi Saleh</a>, locals lived in domed-shaped dwellings in the king's section of the city, surrounded by a great enclosure. Traders lived in stone houses in a section which possessed 12 beautiful mosques, as described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Abdullah_al-Bakri" title="Abu Abdullah al-Bakri">al-bakri</a>, with one centered on <a href="/wiki/Friday_prayer" title="Friday prayer">Friday prayer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The king is said to have owned several mansions, one of which was sixty-six feet long, forty-two feet wide, contained seven rooms, was two stories high, and had a staircase; with the walls and chambers filled with sculpture and painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sudano-Sahelian" title="Sudano-Sahelian">Sahelian architecture</a> initially grew from the two cities of <a href="/wiki/Djenn%C3%A9" title="Djenné">Djenné</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sankore" title="Sankore">Sankore Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a>, constructed from mud on timber, was similar in style to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Djenn%C3%A9" title="Great Mosque of Djenné">Great Mosque of Djenné</a>. The rise of kingdoms in the West African coastal region produced architecture which drew on indigenous traditions, utilizing wood, mud-brick and adobe. Though later acquiring Islamic influences, the style also had roots in local pre-Islamic building styles, such as those found in ancient settlements like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jenne-Jeno" title="Jenne-Jeno">Jenne-Jeno</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dia,_Mali" title="Dia, Mali">Dia, Mali</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dhar_Tichitt" title="Dhar Tichitt">Dhar Tichitt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> some of which employed a traditional sahelian style of cylindrical mud brick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAC_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-AAC-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopian">Ethiopian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ethiopian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 228px"><div style="width: 228px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 226px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bet_Giyorgis_(5498439545).jpg" title="Large, monolithic churches such as the Church of Saint George (Lalibela), were hewn out of the ground in Ethiopia, late 12th or early 13th century"><img alt="Large, monolithic churches such as the Church of Saint George (Lalibela), were hewn out of the ground in Ethiopia, late 12th or early 13th century" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Bet_Giyorgis_%285498439545%29.jpg/339px-Bet_Giyorgis_%285498439545%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Bet_Giyorgis_%285498439545%29.jpg/509px-Bet_Giyorgis_%285498439545%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Bet_Giyorgis_%285498439545%29.jpg/677px-Bet_Giyorgis_%285498439545%29.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Large, monolithic churches such as the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Saint_George,_Lalibela" title="Church of Saint George, Lalibela">Church of Saint George (Lalibela)</a>, were hewn out of the ground in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, late 12th or early 13th century
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Ethiopia" title="Architecture of Ethiopia">Ethiopian architecture</a> (including modern-day <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>) expanded from the Aksumite style and incorporated new traditions with the expansion of the Ethiopian state. Styles incorporated more wood and rounder structures in domestic architecture in the center of the country and the south, and these stylistic influences were manifested in the construction of churches and monasteries. Throughout the medieval period, Aksumite architecture and influences and its monolithic tradition persisted, with its influence strongest in the early medieval (Late Aksumite) and Zagwe periods (when the <a href="/wiki/Rock-cut_architecture" title="Rock-cut architecture">rock-cut</a> <a href="/wiki/Monolithic_church" title="Monolithic church">monolithic churches</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lalibela" title="Lalibela">Lalibela</a> were carved). Throughout the medieval period, and especially from the 10th to 12th centuries, churches were hewn out of rock throughout Ethiopia, especially during the northernmost region of <a href="/wiki/Tigray_Province" title="Tigray Province">Tigray</a>, which was the heart of the Aksumite Empire. The most famous example of Ethiopian rock-hewn architecture are the eleven monolithic churches of Lalibela, carved out of the red volcanic tuff found around the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> During the early modern period in Ethiopia, the absorption of new diverse influences such as Baroque, Arab, Turkish and <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_people" title="Gujarati people">Gujarati</a> style began with the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> missionaries in the 16th and 17th centuries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceanic_art_and_architecture" title="Oceanic art and architecture">Oceanic art and architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Little_world,_Aichi_prefecture_-_House_of_Yap_in_Micronesia.jpg" title="Traditional house in Micronesia, unknown date"><img alt="Traditional house in Micronesia, unknown date" data-file-height="3096" data-file-width="4128" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Little_world%2C_Aichi_prefecture_-_House_of_Yap_in_Micronesia.jpg/300px-Little_world%2C_Aichi_prefecture_-_House_of_Yap_in_Micronesia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Little_world%2C_Aichi_prefecture_-_House_of_Yap_in_Micronesia.jpg/451px-Little_world%2C_Aichi_prefecture_-_House_of_Yap_in_Micronesia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Little_world%2C_Aichi_prefecture_-_House_of_Yap_in_Micronesia.jpg/600px-Little_world%2C_Aichi_prefecture_-_House_of_Yap_in_Micronesia.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Traditional house in <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a>, unknown date
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Most Oceanic buildings consist of <a href="/wiki/Hut" title="Hut">huts</a>, made of wood and other vegetal materials. <a href="/wiki/Oceanian_art" title="Oceanian art">Art</a> and architecture have often been closely connected—for example, storehouses and meetinghouses are often decorated with elaborate carvings—and so they are presented together in this discussion. The architecture of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> Islands was varied and sometimes large in scale. Buildings reflected the structure and preoccupations of the societies that constructed them, with considerable symbolic detail. Technically, most buildings in <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> were no more than simple assemblages of poles held together with cane lashings; only in the Caroline Islands were complex methods of joining and pegging known. Fakhua shen, Taboa shen and Kuhua shen (the shen triplets) designed the first oceanian architecture.
</p><p>An important Oceanic archaeological site is <a href="/wiki/Nan_Madol" title="Nan Madol">Nan Madol</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Federated_States_of_Micronesia" title="Federated States of Micronesia">Federated States of Micronesia</a>. Nan Madol was the ceremonial and political seat of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saudeleur_Dynasty" title="Saudeleur Dynasty">Saudeleur Dynasty</a>, which united Pohnpei's estimated 25,000 people until about 1628.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ayres_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ayres-91">[91]</a></sup> Set apart between the main island of <a href="/wiki/Pohnpei" title="Pohnpei">Pohnpei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temwen_Island" title="Temwen Island">Temwen Island</a>, it was a scene of human activity as early as the first or second century AD. By the 8th or 9th century, islet construction had started, with construction of the distinctive <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megalithic architecture</a> beginning 1180–1200 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCoy_et_al_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy_et_al-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic">Islamic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Islamic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a></div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 219.33333333333px"><div style="width: 219.33333333333px">

<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Due to the extent of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Islamic conquests</a>, Islamic architecture encompasses a wide range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architectural_styles" title="Architectural styles">architectural styles</a> from the foundation of Islam (7th century) to the present day. Early Islamic architecture was influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_architecture" title="Roman architecture">Roman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_architecture" title="Persian architecture">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Mesopotamia" title="Architecture of Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> architecture and all other lands which the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Early Muslim conquests</a> conquered in the 7th and 8th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krautheimer_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krautheimer-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Further east, it was also influenced by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_India" title="Architecture of India">Indian architecture</a> as Islam spread to Southeast Asia. This wide and long history has given rise to many local architectural styles, including but not limited to: <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_architecture" title="Abbasid architecture">Abbasid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_architecture" title="Persian architecture">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moorish_architecture" title="Moorish architecture">Moorish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_architecture" title="Fatimid architecture">Fatimid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_architecture" title="Mamluk architecture">Mamluk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture" title="Ottoman architecture">Ottoman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Islamic_architecture" title="Indo-Islamic architecture">Indo-Islamic</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal</a>), <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Bengal" title="Architecture of Bengal">Medieval Bengali</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_mosques" title="Chinese mosques">Sino-Islamic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sudano-Sahelian_architecture" title="Sudano-Sahelian architecture">Sahelian</a> architecture.
</p><p>Some distinctive structures in Islamic architecture are <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrasas</a>, tombs, palaces, <a href="/wiki/Hammam" title="Hammam">baths</a>, and forts. Notable types of Islamic religious architecture include <a href="/wiki/Hypostyle" title="Hypostyle">hypostyle</a> mosques, domed mosques and mausoleums, structures with vaulted <a href="/wiki/Iwan" title="Iwan">iwans</a>, and madrasas built around central courtyards. In secular architecture, major examples of preserved historic palaces include the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="Topkapı Palace">Topkapi Palace</a>. Islam does not encourage the worship of idols; therefore the architecture tends to be decorated with <a href="/wiki/Arabic_calligraphy" title="Arabic calligraphy">Arabic calligraphy</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Qur'anic</a> verses or other poetry) and with more abstract motifs such as <a href="/wiki/Islamic_geometric_patterns" title="Islamic geometric patterns">geometric patterns</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Muqarnas" title="Muqarnas">muqarnas</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Arabesque" title="Arabesque">arabesques</a>, as opposed to illustrations of scenes and stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:24_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-:24-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="European">European</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: European">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Medieval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_architecture" title="Medieval architecture">Medieval architecture</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_architecture" title="Norse architecture">Norse architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_the_Tarnovo_Artistic_School" title="Architecture of the Tarnovo Artistic School">Architecture of the Tarnovo Artistic School</a></div>
<p>Surviving examples of medieval secular architecture mainly served for defense across various parts of Europe. <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">Castles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortified_wall" title="Fortified wall">fortified walls</a> provide the most notable remaining non-religious examples of medieval architecture. New types of civic, military, as well as religious buildings of new styles begin to pop up in this region during this period.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine">Byzantine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Byzantine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 114.66666666667px"><div style="width: 114.66666666667px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px"><div style="width: 227.33333333333px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Church_of_Panagia_Kapnikarea" title="Church of Panagia Kapnikarea">Kapnikarea</a> (Athens), 1050<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones201490_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones201490-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Byzantine architects built city walls, palaces, hippodromes, bridges, <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(bridge)" title="Aqueduct (bridge)">aqueducts</a>, and churches. They built many types of churches, including the <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> (the most widespread type, and the one that reached the greatest development). After the early period, the most common layout was the <a href="/wiki/Cross-in-square" title="Cross-in-square">cross-in-square</a> with five domes, also found in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novgorod" title="Novgorod">Novgorod</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a>, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>. Through modifications and adaptations of local inspiration, the Byzantine style will be used as the main source of inspiration for architectural styles in all <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> For example, in Romania, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%A2ncovenesc_style" title="Brâncovenesc style">Brâncovenesc style</a> is highly based on Byzantine architecture, but also has individual Romanian characteristics.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:191px;max-width:191px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:126px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/284px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg/378px-Hagia_Sophia_Mars_2013.jpg 2x" width="189"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:97px;max-width:97px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:126px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg/95px-Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg/143px-Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg/190px-Istanbul_Oct_2019_12_20_46_794000.jpeg 2x" width="95"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:151px;max-width:151px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:86px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2429" data-file-width="4176" decoding="async" height="87" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/224px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg/298px-Deesis_mosaic_Hagia_Sophia.jpg 2x" width="149"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:137px;max-width:137px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:86px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2007" data-file-width="3132" decoding="async" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/135px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/203px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg/270px-Hagia_Sophia_Southwestern_entrance_mosaics_2.jpg 2x" width="135"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>), 537, by <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201962_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201962-108">[108]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Just as the <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a> is the most famous building of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_architecture" title="Ancient Greek architecture">Ancient Greek architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> remains the iconic church of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christianity" title="Orthodox Christianity">Orthodox Christianity</a>.  In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_temple" title="Greek temple">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple" title="Roman temple">Roman temples</a>, the exterior was the most important part of the temple, where sacrifices were made; the interior, where the cult statue of the deity to whom the temple was built was kept, often had limited access by the general public. But Christian liturgies are held in the interior of the churches, Byzantine exteriors usually having little if any ornamentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Byzantine architecture often featured marble columns, <a href="/wiki/Coffer" title="Coffer">coffered</a> ceilings and sumptuous decoration, including the extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a> with <a href="/wiki/Gold_ground" title="Gold ground">golden backgrounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201917_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201917-110">[110]</a></sup> The building material used by Byzantine architects was no longer marble, which was very appreciated by the Ancient Greeks. They used mostly stone and brick, and also thin <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> sheets for windows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Mosaics were used to cover brick walls, and any other surface where <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> wouldn't resist. Good examples of mosaics from the proto-Byzantine era are in <a href="/wiki/Hagios_Demetrios" title="Hagios Demetrios">Hagios Demetrios</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a> (Greece), the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Sant%27Apollinare_Nuovo" title="Basilica of Sant'Apollinare Nuovo">Basilica of Sant'Apollinare Nuovo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Vitale" title="Basilica of San Vitale">Basilica of San Vitale</a>, both in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> (Italy), and <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanesque">Romanesque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Romanesque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 227.33333333333px"><div style="width: 227.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 225.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Durham_Cathedral_Nave.jpg" title="Interior of the Durham Cathedral (Durham, UK), 1093-1133[114]"><img alt="Interior of the Durham Cathedral (Durham, UK), 1093-1133[114]" data-file-height="5791" data-file-width="8687" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Durham_Cathedral_Nave.jpg/338px-Durham_Cathedral_Nave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Durham_Cathedral_Nave.jpg/507px-Durham_Cathedral_Nave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Durham_Cathedral_Nave.jpg/675px-Durham_Cathedral_Nave.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 105.33333333333px"><div style="width: 105.33333333333px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The term 'Romanesque' is rooted in the 19th century, when it was coined to describe medieval churches built from the 10th to 12th century, before the rise of steeply pointed arches, <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttresses</a> and other Gothic elements. For 19th-century critics, the Romanesque reflected the architecture of stonemasons who evidently admired the heavy barrel vaults and intricate carved <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)" title="Capital (architecture)">capitals</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Romans</a>, but whose own architecture was considered derivative and degenerate, lacking the sophistication of their classical models.
</p><p>Scholars in the 21st century are less inclined to understand the architecture of this period as a 'failure' to reproduce the achievements of the past, and are far more likely to recognise its profusion of experimental forms, as a series of creative new inventions. At the time, however, research has questioned the value of Romanesque as a stylistic term. On the surface, it provides a convenient designation for buildings that share a common vocabulary of rounded <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">arches</a> and thick stone <a href="/wiki/Masonry" title="Masonry">masonry</a>, and appear in between the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance#Carolingian_architecture" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian revival of classical antiquity</a> in the 9th century and the swift evolution of Gothic architecture after the second half of the 12th century. One problem, however, is that the term encompasses a broad array of regional variations, some with closer links to Rome than others. It should also be noted that the distinction between Romanesque architecture and its immediate predecessors and followers is not at all clear. There is little evidence that medieval viewers were concerned with the stylistic distinctions that we observe today, making the slow evolution of medieval architecture difficult to separate into neat chronological categories. Nevertheless, Romanesque remains a useful word despite its limitations, because it reflects a period of intensive building activity that maintained some continuity with the classical past, but freely reinterpreted ancient forms in a new distinctive manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014148,_149_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014148,_149-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Romanesque cathedrals can be differentiated pretty easy from Gothic and Byzantine ones, since they are characterized by the wide use of thick piers and columns, round arches and severity. Here, the possibilities of the round-arch arcade in both a structural and a spatial sense were once again exploited to the full. Unlike the sharp pointed arch of the later Gothic, the Romanesque round arch required the support of massive piers and columns. In comparison to Byzantine churches, Romanesque ones tend to lack complex ornamentation both on the exterior and interior. An example of this is the <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9rigueux_Cathedral" title="Périgueux Cathedral">Périgueux Cathedral</a> (<a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9rigueux" title="Périgueux">Périgueux</a>, France), built in the early 12th century and designed on the model of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Mark%27s_Basilica" title="St. Mark's Basilica">St. Mark's Basilica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, but lacking mosaics, leaving its interior very austere and minimalistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkins201423,_25_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkins201423,_25-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gothic">Gothic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Gothic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

</ul>
<p>Gothic architecture began with a series of experiments, which were conducted to fulfil specific requests by patrons and to accommodate the ever-growing number of <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrims</a> visiting sites that housed precious relics. Pilgrims in the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">high Middle Ages</a> (circa 1000 to 1250 AD) increasingly travelled to well-known pilgrimage sites, but also to local sites where local and national saints were reputed to have performed miracles. The churches and monasteries housing important <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> therefore wanted to heighten the popularity of their respective saints and build appropriate shrines for them. These shrines were not merely gem-encrusted reliquaries, but more importantly took the form of powerful architectural settings characterised by coloured light emitting from the large areas of <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a>. The use of stained glass, however, is not the only defining element of Gothic architecture and neither are the <a href="/wiki/Pointed_arch_(architecture)" title="Pointed arch (architecture)">pointed arch</a>, the ribbed <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vault</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rose_window" title="Rose window">rose window</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a>, as many of these elements were used in one way or another in preceding architectural traditions. It was rather the combination and constant refinement of these elements, along with the quick response to the rapidly changing building techniques of the time, that fuelled the Gothic movement in architecture.
</p>

src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg/223px-Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg/335px-Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg/446px-Sainte_Chapelle_Interior_Stained_Glass.jpg 2x" width="223"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:114px;max-width:114px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:111px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paris_-_MNMA_Vitrail_SC_02.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paris_-_MNMA_Vitrail_SC_02.jpg/112px-Paris_-_MNMA_Vitrail_SC_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paris_-_MNMA_Vitrail_SC_02.jpg/168px-Paris_-_MNMA_Vitrail_SC_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Paris_-_MNMA_Vitrail_SC_02.jpg/224px-Paris_-_MNMA_Vitrail_SC_02.jpg 2x" width="112"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:174px;max-width:174px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:111px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ste_Chapelle_Basse_s.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1297" data-file-width="1999" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Ste_Chapelle_Basse_s.jpg/172px-Ste_Chapelle_Basse_s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Ste_Chapelle_Basse_s.jpg/258px-Ste_Chapelle_Basse_s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Ste_Chapelle_Basse_s.jpg/344px-Ste_Chapelle_Basse_s.jpg 2x" width="172"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Chapelle" title="Sainte-Chapelle">Sainte-Chapelle</a> (Paris) and a few details, 1243-1248, by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Montreuil" title="Pierre de Montreuil">Pierre de Montreuil</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Consequently, it is difficult to point to one element or the exact place where Gothic first emerged; however, it is traditional to initiate a discussion of Gothic architecture with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_St_Denis" title="Basilica of St Denis">Basilica of St Denis</a> (circa 1135–1344) and its patrons, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbot_Suger" title="Abbot Suger">Abbot Suger</a>, who began to rebuild the west front and the choir of the church. As he wrote in his <i>De Administratione</i>, the old building could no longer accommodate the large volumes of pilgrims who were coming to venerate the relics of St Denis, and the solution for this twofold: a west <a href="/wiki/Fa%C3%A7ade" title="Façade">façade</a> with three large <a href="/wiki/Portal_(architecture)" title="Portal (architecture)">portals</a> and the innovative new <a href="/wiki/Choir_(architecture)" title="Choir (architecture)">choir</a>, which combined an <a href="/wiki/Ambulatory" title="Ambulatory">ambulatory</a> with radiating <a href="/wiki/Chapel" title="Chapel">chapels</a> that were unique as they were not separated by walls. Instead a row of slim columns was inserted between the chapels and the choir arcade to support the rib vaults. The result enabled visitors to circulate around the altar and come within reach of the relics without actually disrupting the altar space, while also experiencing the large stained-glass windows within the chapels. As confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Suger" title="Suger">Suger</a>, the desire for more stained-glass was not necessarily to bring more daylight into the building but rather to fill the space with a continuous ray of colorful light, rather like mosaics or precious stones, which would make the wall vanish. The demand for ever more stained-glass windows and the search for techniques that would support them are constant throughout the development of Gothic architecture, as is evident in the writings of Suger, who was fascinated by the mystical quality of such lighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014148,_149_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014148,_149-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, Italy consisted of many states, and intense rivalry between them generated an increase in technical and artistic developments. The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">Medici Family</a>, an Italian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banking_family" title="Banking family">banking family</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_dynasty" title="Political dynasty">political dynasty</a>, is famous for its financial support of Renaissance art and architecture.
</p><p>The period began in around 1452, when the architect and humanist <a href="/wiki/Leon_Battista_Alberti" title="Leon Battista Alberti">Leon Battista Alberti</a> (1404-1472) completed his treatise <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Re_Aedificatoria" title="De Re Aedificatoria">De Re Aedificatoria</a></i> (<i>On the Art of Building</i>) after studying the ancient ruins of Rome and <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Architectura" title="De Architectura">De Architectura</a></i>. His writings covered numerous subjects, including history, town planning, engineering, sacred geometry, <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> and philosophies of beauty, and set out the key elements of architecture and its ideal proportions. In the last decades of the 15th century, artists and architects began to visit Rome to study the ruins, especially the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a>. They left behind precious records of their studies in the form of drawings. While humanist interest in Rome had been building up over more than a century (dating back at least to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francesco_Petrarca" title="Francesco Petrarca">Petrarch</a> in the 14th century), antiquarian considerations of monuments had focused on literary, epigraphic and historical information rather than on the physical remains. Although some artists and architects, such as <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a> (1377-1446), <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a> (circa 1386–1466) and Leon Battista Alberti, are reported to have made studies of Roman sculpture and ruins, almost no direct evidence of this work survives. By the 1480s, prominent architects, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francesco_di_Giorgio" title="Francesco di Giorgio">Francesco di Giorgio</a> (1439-1502) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Giuliano_da_Sabgallo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Giuliano da Sabgallo (page does not exist)">Giuliano da Sabgallo</a> (circa 1445–1516), were making numerous studies of ancient monuments, undertaken in ways that demonstrated that the process of transforming the model into a new design had already begun. In many cases, drawing ruins in their fragmentary state necessitated a leap of imagination, as Francesco himself readily admitted in his annotation to his reconstruction of the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Hill" title="Capitoline Hill">Campidoglio</a>, noting 'largely imagined by me, since very little can be understood from the ruins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014196_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014196-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Soon, grand buildings were constructed in Florence using the new style, like the <a href="/wiki/Pazzi_Chapel" title="Pazzi Chapel">Pazzi Chapel</a> (1441-1478) or the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Pitti" title="Palazzo Pitti">Palazzo Pitti</a> (1458-1464). The Renaissance begun in Italy, but slowly spread to other parts of Europe, with varying interpretations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201926_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201926-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since Renaissance art is an attempt of reviving <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a>'s culture, it uses pretty much the same ornaments as the Ancient Greek and Roman. However, because most if not all resources that Renaissance artists had were <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Roman</a>, Renaissance architecture and applied arts widely use certain motifs and ornaments that are specific to Ancient Rome. The most iconic one is the <a href="/wiki/Margent" title="Margent">margent</a>, a vertical arrangement of flowers, leaves or hanging vines, used at <a href="/wiki/Pilaster" title="Pilaster">pilasters</a>. Another ornament associated with the Renaissance is the round <a href="/wiki/Medallion_(architecture)" title="Medallion (architecture)">medallion</a>, containing a profile of a person, similar with Ancient <a href="/wiki/Cameo_(carving)" title="Cameo (carving)">cameos</a>. Renaissance, Baroque, Rococo, and other post-medieval styles use <a href="/wiki/Putto" title="Putto">putti</a> (chubby baby angels) much more often compared to Greco-Roman art and architecture. An ornament reintroduced during the Renaissance, that was of Ancient Roman descent, that will also be used in later styles, is the <a href="/wiki/Cartouche_(design)" title="Cartouche (design)">cartouche</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Oval" title="Oval">oval</a> or oblong design with a slightly <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/convex" title="wikt:convex">convex</a> surface, typically edged with ornamental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scrollwork" title="Scrollwork">scrollwork</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide">Worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Worldwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baroque">Baroque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Baroque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 211.33333333333px"><div style="width: 211.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 209.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palacio_Real,_%C3%81msterdam,_Pa%C3%ADses_Bajos,_2016-05-30,_DD_07-09_HDR.jpg" title="Royal Palace of Amsterdam (Amsterdam, The Netherlands), 1648-1665, by Jacob van Campen"><img alt="Royal Palace of Amsterdam (Amsterdam, The Netherlands), 1648-1665, by Jacob van Campen" data-file-height="5634" data-file-width="7846" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Palacio_Real%2C_%C3%81msterdam%2C_Pa%C3%ADses_Bajos%2C_2016-05-30%2C_DD_07-09_HDR.jpg/314px-Palacio_Real%2C_%C3%81msterdam%2C_Pa%C3%ADses_Bajos%2C_2016-05-30%2C_DD_07-09_HDR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Palacio_Real%2C_%C3%81msterdam%2C_Pa%C3%ADses_Bajos%2C_2016-05-30%2C_DD_07-09_HDR.jpg/471px-Palacio_Real%2C_%C3%81msterdam%2C_Pa%C3%ADses_Bajos%2C_2016-05-30%2C_DD_07-09_HDR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Palacio_Real%2C_%C3%81msterdam%2C_Pa%C3%ADses_Bajos%2C_2016-05-30%2C_DD_07-09_HDR.jpg/627px-Palacio_Real%2C_%C3%81msterdam%2C_Pa%C3%ADses_Bajos%2C_2016-05-30%2C_DD_07-09_HDR.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Royal_Palace_of_Amsterdam" title="Royal Palace of Amsterdam">Royal Palace of Amsterdam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Netherlands" title="The Netherlands">The Netherlands</a>), 1648-1665, by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_van_Campen" title="Jacob van Campen">Jacob van Campen</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The Baroque emerged from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter_Reformation" title="Counter Reformation">Counter Reformation</a> as an attempt by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> in Rome to convey its power and to emphasize the magnificence of God. The Baroque and its late variant the <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> were the first truly global styles in the arts. Dominating more than two centuries of art and architecture in Europe, Latin America and beyond from circa 1580 to circa 1800. Born in the painting studios of <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> in the 1580s and 1590s, and in Roman sculptural and architectural ateliers in the second and third decades of the 17th century, the Baroque spread swiftly throughout <a href="/wiki/Italian_Baroque_architecture" title="Italian Baroque architecture">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Baroque_architecture" title="Spanish Baroque architecture">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture_in_Portugal" title="Baroque architecture in Portugal">Portugal</a>, Flanders, <a href="/wiki/French_Baroque_architecture" title="French Baroque architecture">France</a>, the Netherlands, England, Scandinavia, and Russia, as well as to central and eastern European centres from <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> (Germany) to <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French</a> empires and the Dutch treading network had a leading role in spreading the two styles into the Americas and colonial Africa and Asia, to places such as <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey20124_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey20124-144">[144]</a></sup> Due to its spread in regions with different architectural traditions, multiple kinds of Baroque appeared based on location, different in some aspects, but similar overall. For example, French Baroque appeared severe and detached by comparison, preempting <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassicism</a> and the architecture of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014223_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014223-134">[134]</a></sup> Hybrid Native American/European Baroque architecture first appeared in South America (as opposed to Mexico) in the late 17th century, after the indigenous symbols and styles that characterize this unusual variant of Baroque had been kept alive over the preceding century in other media, a very good example of this being the <a class="external text" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Jesuit_Church,_Arequipa">Jesuir Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arequipa" title="Arequipa">Arequipa</a> (Peru).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey2012364_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey2012364-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" 2x" width="167"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Gardens_of_Versailles" title="Gardens of Versailles">The gardens</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The first Baroque buildings were cathedrals, churches and monasteries, soon joined by civic buildings, mansions, and palaces. Being characterized by dynamism, for the first time walls, façades and interiors curved,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201929_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201929-147">[147]</a></sup> a good example being <a href="/wiki/San_Carlo_alle_Quattro_Fontane" title="San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane">San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane</a> in Rome. Baroque architects took the basic elements of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance architecture</a>, including domes and colonnades, and made them higher, grander, more decorated, and more dramatic. The interior effects were often achieved with the use of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadratura" title="Quadratura">quadratura</a></i>,  or <i><a href="/wiki/Trompe-l%27%C5%93il" title="Trompe-l'œil">trompe-l'œil</a></i> painting combined with sculpture: the eye is drawn upward, giving the illusion that one is looking into the heavens. Clusters of sculpted angels and painted figures crowd the ceiling. Light was also used for dramatic effect; it streamed down from cupolas and was reflected from an abundance of gilding. <a href="/wiki/Solomonic_column" title="Solomonic column">Solomonic columns</a> were often used, to give an illusion of upwards motion and other decorative elements occupied every available space. In Baroque palaces, grand stairways became a central element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Besides architecture, Baroque painting and sculpture are characterized by dynamism too. This is in contrast with how static and peaceful <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_art" title="Renaissance art">Renaissance art</a> is.
</p><p>Besides the building itself, the space where it was placed had a role too. Both Baroque and Rococo buildings try to seize viewers' attention and to dominate their surroundings, whether on a small scale such as the <a href="/wiki/San_Carlo_alle_Quattro_Fontane" title="San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane">San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane</a> in Rome, or on a massive one, like the new facade of the <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_Compostela_Cathedral" title="Santiago de Compostela Cathedral">Santiago de Compostela Cathedral</a>, designed to tower over the city. A manifestation of power and authority on the grandest scale, Baroque urban planing and renewal was promoted by the church and the state alike. It was the first era since antiquity to experience mass migration into cities, and urban planners took idealistic measures to regulate them. The most notable early example was <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Fontana" title="Domenico Fontana">Domenico Fontana</a>'s restructuring of Rome's street plan of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_V" title="Pope Sixtus V">Pope Sixtus V</a>. Architects had experimented with idealized city schemes since the early Renaissance, examples being <a href="/wiki/Leon_Battista_Alberti" title="Leon Battista Alberti">Leon Battista Alberti</a> (1404-1472) planning a centralized model city, with streets leading to a central piazza, or <a href="/wiki/Filarete" title="Filarete">Filarete</a> (Antonio di Pietro Aver(u)lino, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1400</span>-<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1469</span>) designing a round city named <a href="/wiki/Sforzinda" title="Sforzinda">Sforzinda</a> (1451-1456) that he based on parts of the human body in the idea that a healthy city should reflect the physiognomy of its inhabitants. However, none of these idealistic cities has ever been built. In fact, few such projects were put into practice in Europe as new cities were prohibitively costly and existing urban areas, with existing churches and palaces, could not be demolished. Only in the Americas, where architects often had a clean space to work with, were such cities possible, as in <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a> (<a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> (<a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>). The earliest Baroque ideal city is <a href="/wiki/Zamo%C5%9B%C4%87" title="Zamość">Zamość</a>, built north-east of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krakow" title="Krakow">Krakow</a> (<a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>) by the Italian architect <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Morando" title="Bernardo Morando">Bernardo Morando</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1540</span>-1600), being a centralized town focusing on a square with radiating streets. Where entire cities could not be rebuilt, patrons and architects compensated by creating spacious and symmetrical squares, often with avenues and radiating out at perpendicular angles and focusing on a fountain, statue or <a href="/wiki/Obelisk" title="Obelisk">obelisk</a>. A good example of this is the <a href="/wiki/Place_des_Vosges" title="Place des Vosges">Place des Vosges</a> (formerly Place Royale), commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV</a> probably after plans by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptiste_du_Cerceau" title="Baptiste du Cerceau">Baptiste du Cerceau</a> (1545-1590). The most famous Baroque space in the world is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gianlorenzo_Bernini" title="Gianlorenzo Bernini">Gianlorenzo Bernini</a>'s <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Square" title="St. Peter's Square">St. Peter's Square</a> in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey2012205,_206,_207_&amp;_208_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey2012205,_206,_207_&amp;_208-149">[149]</a></sup> Similar with ideal urban planning, <a href="/wiki/Baroque_garden" title="Baroque garden">Baroque gardens</a> are characterized by straight and readapting avenues, with geometric spaces.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rococo">Rococo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Rococo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocaille" title="Rocaille">Rocaille</a></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The name <i>Rococo</i> derives from the French word <i><a href="/wiki/Rocaille" title="Rocaille">rocaille</a></i>, which describes shell-covered rock-work, and <i>coquille</i>, meaning seashell. Rococo architecture is fancy and fluid, accentuating asymmetry, with an abundant use of curves, scrolls, gilding and ornaments. The style enjoyed great popularity with the ruling elite of Europe during the first half of the 18th century. It developed in France out of a new fashion in interior decoration, and spread across Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201930_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201930-153">[153]</a></sup> Domestic Rococo abandoned Baroque's high moral tone, its weighty allegories and its obsession with legitimacy: in fact, its abstract forms and carefree, pastoral subjects related more to notions of refuge and joy that created a more forgiving atmosphere for polite conversations. Rococo rooms are typically smaller than their Baroque counterparts, reflecting a movement towards domestic intimacy. Even the grander salons used for entertaining were more modest in scale, as social events involved smaller numbers of guests.
</p><p>Characteristic of the style were Rocaille motifs derived from the shells, icicles and rock-work or grotto decoration. Rocaille arabesques were mostly abstract forms, laid out symmetrically over and around architectural frames. A favourite motif was the scallop shell, whose top scrolls echoed the basic S and C framework scrolls of the <a href="/wiki/Arabesque" title="Arabesque">arabesques</a> and whose sinuous ridges echoed the general curvilinearity of the room decoration. While few Rococo exteriors were built in France, a number of Rococo churches are found in southern Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECole2002270_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECole2002270-154">[154]</a></sup> Other widely-user motifs in decorative arts and interior architecture include: <a href="/wiki/Acanthus_(ornament)" title="Acanthus (ornament)">acanthus</a> and other leaves, birds, bouquets of flowers, fruits, elements associated with love (<a href="/wiki/Putto" title="Putto">putti</a>, quivers with arrows ans arrowed hearts) <a href="/wiki/Trophy_of_arms" title="Trophy of arms">trophies of arms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Putto" title="Putto">putti</a>, medallions with faces, many many flowers, and <a href="/wiki/Chinoiserie" title="Chinoiserie">Far Eastern elements</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagodes</a>, dragons, monkeys, bizarre flowers, bamboo, and Chinese people).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Pastel colours were widely used, like light blue, mint green or pink. Rococo designers also loved mirrors (the more the better), an example being the Hall of Mirrors of the <a href="/wiki/Amalienburg" title="Amalienburg">Amalienburg</a> (<a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>, Germany), by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Baptist_Zimmermann" title="Johann Baptist Zimmermann">Johann Baptist Zimmermann</a>. Generally, mirrors are also featured above fireplaces.
</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px"><div style="width: 152px">
