Pflueger">Timothy L. Pflueger</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The interactions between East and West brought on by <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialist</a> exploration have had a long impact on architecture. Because of being something rare and new to Westerners, some non-European styles were really appreciated during the 17th, 18th and 19th centuries. Some nobles and kings built little structures inspired by these styles in the gardens of their palaces, or fully decorated a handful of rooms of palaces in these styles. Because of not fully understanding the origins and principles that govern these exotic ways of building, Europeans sometimes created hybrids of the style which they tried to replicate and what were the trends at that time. A good example of this is <i><a href="/wiki/Chinoiserie" title="Chinoiserie">chinoiserie</a></i>, a Western <a href="/wiki/Decorative_arts" title="Decorative arts">decorative</a> style, popular during the 18 the century, that was heavily inspired by Chinese arts, but also by <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> at the same time. Because traveling to China or other <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far Eastern</a> countries was something hard at that time and so remained mysterious to most Westerners, European imaginations were fuelled by perceptions of Asia as a place of wealth and luxury, and consequently patrons from emperors to merchants vied with each other in adorning their living quarters with Asian goods and decorating them in Asian styles. Where Asian objects were hard to obtain, European craftsmen and painters stepped up to fill the breach, creating a blend of Rococo forms and authentic Asian figures, motifs and techniques.
</p><p>Chinese art wasn't the only foreign style with which Europeans experimented. Another was the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic</a> one. Examples of this include the Garden Mosque of the <a href="/wiki/Schwetzingen_Palace" title="Schwetzingen Palace">Schwetzingen Palace</a> in Germany (the only surviving example of an 18th century European garden mosque), the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Pavilion" title="Royal Pavilion">Royal Pavilion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Moorish_Revival_architecture" title="Moorish Revival architecture">Moorish Revival</a> buildings from the 19th and early 20th centuries, with horseshoe arches and brick patterns. When it come to the Orient, Europeans also had an interest for the culture of Ancient Egypt. Compared to other cases of exoticism, the one with the land of <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaohs</a> is the oldest one, since <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> had this interest during Antiquity. The main periods when <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revival_architecture" title="Egyptian Revival architecture">Egyptian Revival</a> monuments were erected were the early 19th century, with Napoleon's military campaigns in Egypt, and the 1920s, when the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Tutankhamun" title="Tomb of Tutankhamun">Tomb of Tutankhamun</a> was discovered in 1922, which caused an <a href="/wiki/Egyptomania" title="Egyptomania">Egyptomania</a> that lead to <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> sometimes using motifs inspired by Ancient Egypt. During the late 18th and early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism" title="Neoclassicism">Neoclassicism</a> sometimes mixed Greco-Roman elements with Egyptian ones. Because of its association with <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaohs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eternity" title="Eternity">eternity</a>, multiple Egyptian Revival tombs or cemetery entry gates were built in this style. Besides mortuary structures, other buildings in this style include certain <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogues</a>, like the <a href="/wiki/Karlsruhe_Synagogue" title="Karlsruhe Synagogue">Karlsruhe Synagogue</a> or some <a href="/wiki/Empire_style" title="Empire style">Empire</a> monuments built during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nepoleon" title="Nepoleon">Nepoleon</a>, such as the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Portico" title="Portico">portico</a> of the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_Beauharnais" title="Hôtel Beauharnais">Hôtel Beauharnais</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Fontaine_du_Fellah" title="Fontaine du Fellah">Fontaine du Fellah</a>. During the 1920s and 1930s, <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">Pre-Columbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_architecture" title="Mesoamerican architecture">Mesoamerican architecture</a> was of great interest for some American architects, particularly what the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayans</a> built. Several of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lloyd_Wright" title="Frank Lloyd Wright">Frank Lloyd Wright</a>'s California houses were erected in a Mayan Revival style, while other architects combined  Mayan motifs with Art Deco ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkins2014117,_130,_225Bailey2012279,_281Jones2014265Sund2019208,_216,_217,_224,_225_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkins2014117,_130,_225Bailey2012279,_281Jones2014265Sund2019208,_216,_217,_224,_225-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neoclassicism">Neoclassicism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Neoclassicism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet_-_Salle_de_bains_de_Napol%C3%A9on.jpg" title="Napoleon's bath of the Château de Rambouillet (Rambouillet, France), 1806, painted by Godard and Jean Vasserot"><img alt="Napoleon's bath of the Château de Rambouillet (Rambouillet, France), 1806, painted by Godard and Jean Vasserot" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet_-_Salle_de_bains_de_Napol%C3%A9on.jpg/300px-Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet_-_Salle_de_bains_de_Napol%C3%A9on.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet_-_Salle_de_bains_de_Napol%C3%A9on.jpg/451px-Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet_-_Salle_de_bains_de_Napol%C3%A9on.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet_-_Salle_de_bains_de_Napol%C3%A9on.jpg/600px-Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet_-_Salle_de_bains_de_Napol%C3%A9on.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Napoleon's bath of the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Rambouillet" title="Château de Rambouillet">Château de Rambouillet</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rambouillet" title="Rambouillet">Rambouillet</a>, France), 1806, painted by Godard and Jean Vasserot
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 230.66666666667px"><div style="width: 230.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 228.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:150214_Neue_Wache_Berlin.jpg" title="Neue Wache (Berlin), 1816, by Karl Friedrich Schinkel and Salomo Sachs[172]"><img alt="Neue Wache (Berlin), 1816, by Karl Friedrich Schinkel and Salomo Sachs[172]" data-file-height="2995" data-file-width="4563" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/150214_Neue_Wache_Berlin.jpg/343px-150214_Neue_Wache_Berlin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/150214_Neue_Wache_Berlin.jpg/515px-150214_Neue_Wache_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/150214_Neue_Wache_Berlin.jpg/686px-150214_Neue_Wache_Berlin.jpg 2x" width="229"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Neue_Wache" title="Neue Wache">Neue Wache</a> (<a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>), 1816, by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Friedrich_Schinkel" title="Karl Friedrich Schinkel">Karl Friedrich Schinkel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salomo_Sachs" title="Salomo Sachs">Salomo Sachs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 124.66666666667px"><div style="width: 124.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 122.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burns_Monument_(43281676364).jpg" title="Burns Monument (Edinburgh, the UK) 1820-1831, by Thomas Hamilton[173]"><img alt="Burns Monument (Edinburgh, the UK) 1820-1831, by Thomas Hamilton[173]" data-file-height="4888" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Burns_Monument_%2843281676364%29.jpg/184px-Burns_Monument_%2843281676364%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Burns_Monument_%2843281676364%29.jpg/277px-Burns_Monument_%2843281676364%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Burns_Monument_%2843281676364%29.jpg/368px-Burns_Monument_%2843281676364%29.jpg 2x" width="123"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burns_Monument" title="Burns Monument">Burns Monument</a> (<a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, the UK) 1820-1831, by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hamilton_(architect)" title="Thomas Hamilton (architect)">Thomas Hamilton</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkins2014109_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkins2014109-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Neoclassical architecture focused on <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_architecture" title="Ancient Greek architecture">Ancient Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Roman</a> details, plain, white walls and grandeur of scale. Compared to the previous styles, Baroque and Rococo, Neoclassical exteriors tended to be more minimalist, featuring straight and angular lines, but being still ornamented. The style's clean lines and sense of balance and proportion worked well for grand buildings (such as the <a href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on" title="Panthéon">Panthéon</a> in Paris) and for smaller structures alike (such as the <a href="/wiki/Petit_Trianon" title="Petit Trianon">Petit Trianon</a>).
</p><p>Excavations during the 18th century at <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a>, which had both been buried under volcanic ash during the 79 AD eruption of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vesuvius" title="Mount Vesuvius">Mount Vesuvius</a>, inspired a return to order amd rationality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201931_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201931-174">[174]</a></sup> In the mid-18th century, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">antiquity</a> was upheld as a standard for architecture as never before. Neoclassicism was a fundamental investigation of the very bases of architectural form and meaning. In the 1750s, an alliance between archaeological exploration and architectural theory started, which will continue in the 19th century. <a href="/wiki/Marc-Antoine_Laugier" title="Marc-Antoine Laugier">Marc-Antoine Laugier</a> wrote in 1753 that 'Architecture owes all that is perfect to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergdoll20009,_14_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergdoll20009,_14-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" 1.5x, 2x" width="166"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The Cenotaph of Newton, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1784 (never built), by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Louis_Boull%C3%A9e" title="Étienne-Louis Boullée">Étienne-Louis Boullée</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014275_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014275-171">[171]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>The style was adopted by progressive circles in other countries such as Sweden and Russia. <a href="/wiki/Federal_architecture" title="Federal architecture">Federal-style architecture</a> is the name for the classicizing architecture built in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> between c. 1780 and 1830, and particularly from 1785 to 1815. This style shares its name with its era, the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Era" title="Federalist Era">Federal Period</a>. The term is also used in association with furniture design in the United States of the same time period. The style broadly corresponds to the middle-class classicism of <a href="/wiki/Biedermeier" title="Biedermeier">Biedermeier</a> style in the German-speaking lands, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regency_style" title="Regency style">Regency style</a> in Britain and to the French <a href="/wiki/Empire_style" title="Empire style">Empire style</a>. In Central and Eastern Europe, the style is usually referred to as <i>Classicism</i> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Klassizismus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Классицизм</span>), while the newer <a href="/wiki/Revivalism_(architecture)" title="Revivalism (architecture)">Revival styles</a> of the 19th century until today are called neoclassical.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Louis_Boull%C3%A9e" title="Étienne-Louis Boullée">Étienne-Louis Boullée</a> (1728–1799) was a visionary architect of the period. His <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> projects, never built, included a monument to <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1784) in the form of an immense dome, with an <a href="/wiki/Oculus_(architecture)" title="Oculus (architecture)">oculus</a> allowing the light to enter, giving the impression of a sky full of stars. His <a class="external text" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Bibliotheque_nationale_boul.jpg">project for an enlargement of the Royal Library</a> (1785) was even more dramatic, with a gigantic arch sheltering the collection of books. While none of his projects were ever built, the images were widely published and inspired architects of the period to look outside the traditional forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly with the Renaissance and Baroque periods, during the Neoclassical one urban theories of how a good city should be appeared too. <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> writers of the 18th century decried the problems of Paris at that time, the biggest one being the big number of narrow medieval streets crowded with modest houses. <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> openly criticized the failure of the French Royal administration to initiate public works, improve the quality of life in towns, and stimulate the economy. 'It is time for those who rule the most opulent capital in Europe to make it the most comfortable and the most magnificent of cities. There must be public markets, fountains which actually provide water and regular pavements. The narrow and infected streets must be widened, monuments that cannot be seen must be revealed and new ones built for all to see', Voltaire insisted in a polemical essay on 'The Embellishments of Paris' in 1749. In the same year, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=La_Font_de_Saint-Yenne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="La Font de Saint-Yenne (page does not exist)">La Font de Saint-Yenne</a>, criticized how <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a>'s great <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Colonnade" title="Louvre Colonnade">east façade of the Louvre</a>, was all but hidden from views by a dense quarter of modest houses. Voltaire also said that in order to transform Paris into a city that could rival ancient Rome, it was necessary to demolish more than it was to built. 'Our towns are still what they were, a mass of houses crowded together haphazardly without system, planning or design', <a href="/wiki/Marc-Antoine_Laugier" title="Marc-Antoine Laugier">Marc-Antoine Laugier</a> complained in 1753. Writing a decade later, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Patte" title="Pierre Patte">Pierre Patte</a> promoted an urban reform in quest of health, social order, and security, launching at the same time a medical and organic metaphor which compared the operations of urban design to those of the surgeons. With bad air and lack of fresh water its current state was pathological, Patte asserted, calling for fountains to be placed at principal intersections and markets. Squares are recommended promote the circulation of air, and for the same reason houses on the city's bridges should be demolished. He also criticized the location of hospitals next to markets and protested continued burials in overcrowded city churchyards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergdoll200048,_49,_50_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergdoll200048,_49,_50-178">[178]</a></sup> Besides cities, new ideas of how a garden should be appeared in 18th century England, making place for the <a href="/wiki/English_landscape_garden" title="English landscape garden">English landscape garden</a> (aka <i>jardin à l'anglaise</i>), characterized by an idealized view of nature, and the use of Greco-Roman or Gothic ruins, bridges, and other picturesque architecture, designed to recreate an idyllic pastoral landscape. It was the opposite of the symmetrical and geometrically planned <a href="/wiki/Baroque_garden" title="Baroque garden">Baroque garden</a> (aka <i>jardin à la française</i>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revivalism_and_Eclecticism">Revivalism and Eclecticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Revivalism and Eclecticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Revivalism_(architecture)" title="Revivalism (architecture)">Revivalism (architecture)</a></div>

<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 164.66666666667px"><div style="width: 164.66666666667px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px"><div style="width: 152px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 189.33333333333px"><div style="width: 189.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 187.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ankara_asv2021-10_img23_Ziraat_Bank_Museum.jpg" title="First national movement - Ziraat Bank Museum (Ankara, Turkey), 1929, by Giulio Mongeri"><img alt="First national movement - Ziraat Bank Museum (Ankara, Turkey), 1929, by Giulio Mongeri" data-file-height="3987" data-file-width="4984" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Ankara_asv2021-10_img23_Ziraat_Bank_Museum.jpg/281px-Ankara_asv2021-10_img23_Ziraat_Bank_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Ankara_asv2021-10_img23_Ziraat_Bank_Museum.jpg/423px-Ankara_asv2021-10_img23_Ziraat_Bank_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Ankara_asv2021-10_img23_Ziraat_Bank_Museum.jpg/563px-Ankara_asv2021-10_img23_Ziraat_Bank_Museum.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The 19th century was dominated by a wide variety of stylistic revivals, variations, and interpretations. <a href="/wiki/Revivalism_(architecture)" title="Revivalism (architecture)">Revivalism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a> is the use of visual styles that consciously echo the style of a <a href="/wiki/Architectural_style" title="Architectural style">previous architectural era</a>. Modern-day Revival styles can be summarized within <a href="/wiki/New_Classical_architecture" title="New Classical architecture">New Classical architecture</a>, and sometimes under the umbrella term traditional architecture.
</p><p>The idea that architecture might represent the glory of kingdoms can be traced to the dawn of civilisation, but the notion that architecture can bear the stamp of national character is a modern idea, that appeared in the 18th century historical thinking and given political currency in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. As the map of Europe was repeatedly changing, architecture was used to grant the aura of a glorious past to even the most recent nations. In addition to the credo of universal Classicism, two new, and often contradictory, attitudes on historical styles existed in the early 19th century. Pluralism promoted the simultaneous use of the expanded range of style, while Revivalism held that a single historical model was appropriate for modern architecture. Associations between styles and building types appeared, for example: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revival_architecture" title="Egyptian Revival architecture">Egyptian</a> for prisons, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic</a> for churches, or <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Revival_architecture" title="Renaissance Revival architecture">Renaissance Revival</a> for banks and exchanges. These choices were the result of other associations: the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaohs</a> with <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eternity" title="Eternity">eternity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> with <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">Medici family</a> with the rise of banking and modern commerce.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_of_Devonpart,_near_Plymouth,_by_John_Foulston,_1820s,_including_an_%27Egyptian%27_library,_a_%27Hindoo%27_nonconformist_chapel,_a_%27primitive_Doric%27_town_hall,_and_a_street_of_houses_with_a_Roman_Corinthian_order.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="209" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/View_of_Devonpart%2C_near_Plymouth%2C_by_John_Foulston%2C_1820s%2C_including_an_%27Egyptian%27_library%2C_a_%27Hindoo%27_nonconformist_chapel%2C_a_%27primitive_Doric%27_town_hall%2C_and_a_street_of_houses_with_a_Roman_Corinthian_order.jpg/450px-thumbnail.jpg //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/View_of_Devonpart%2C_near_Plymouth%2C_by_John_Foulston%2C_1820s%2C_including_an_%27Egyptian%27_library%2C_a_%27Hindoo%27_nonconformist_chapel%2C_a_%27primitive_Doric%27_town_hall%2C_and_a_street_of_houses_with_a_Roman_Corinthian_order.jpg/600px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_of_Devonpart,_near_Plymouth,_by_John_Foulston,_1820s,_including_an_%27Egyptian%27_library,_a_%27Hindoo%27_nonconformist_chapel,_a_%27primitive_Doric%27_town_hall,_and_a_street_of_houses_with_a_Roman_Corinthian_order.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/Devonport,_Plymouth" title="Devonport, Plymouth">Devonpart</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a> (the UK), by <a href="/wiki/John_Foulston" title="John Foulston">John Foulston</a>, 1820s, including an '<a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revival_architecture" title="Egyptian Revival architecture">Egyptian</a>' library, a 'Hindoo' nonconformist chapel, a 'primitive <a href="/wiki/Doric_order" title="Doric order">Doric</a>' town hall, and a street of houses with a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian order</a></div></div></div>
<p>Whether their choice was <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Classical</a>, medieval, or <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Revival_architecture" title="Renaissance Revival architecture">Renaissance</a>, all revivalists shared the strategy of advocating a particular style based on national history, one of the great enterprises of historians in the early 19th century. Only one historic period was claimed to be the only one capable of providing models grounded in national traditions, institutions, or values. Issues of style became matters of state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000139,_140,_141_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000139,_140,_141-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most well-known Revivalist style is the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic Revival</a> one, that appeared in the mid-18th century in the houses of a number of wealthy antiquarians in England, a notable example being the <a href="/wiki/Strawberry_Hill_House" title="Strawberry Hill House">Strawberry Hill House</a>. German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantism" title="Romantism">Romantic</a> writers and architects were the first to promote Gothic as a powerful expression of national character, and in turn use it as a symbol of national identity in territories still divided. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Herder" title="Johann Gottfried Herder">Johann Gottfried Herder</a> posed the question 'Why should we always imitate foreigners, as if we were Greeks or Romans?'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000139–142,_145_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000139–142,_145-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>In art and architecture history, the term <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalism</a> refers to the works of the Western artists who specialized in Oriental subjects, produced from their travels in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, during the 19th century. In that time, artists and scholars were described as Orientalists, especially in France.
</p><p>In India, during the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>, a new style, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Saracenic_architecture" title="Indo-Saracenic architecture">Indo-Saracenic</a>, (also known as <b>Indo-Gothic</b>, <b>Mughal-Gothic</b>, <b>Neo-Mughal</b>, or <b>Hindoo style</b>) was getting developed, which incorporated varying degrees of Indian elements into the Western European style. The <a href="/wiki/Churches_and_convents_of_Goa" title="Churches and convents of Goa">Churches and convents of Goa</a> are another example of the blending of traditional Indian styles with western European architectural styles. Most Indo-Saracenic public buildings were constructed between 1858 and 1947, with the peaking at 1880.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>  The style has been described as "part of a 19th-century movement to project themselves as the natural successors of the Mughals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>  They were often built for modern functions such as transport stations, government offices, and law courts. It is much more evident in British power centres in the subcontinent like <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chennai" title="Chennai">Chennai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beaux-Arts">Beaux-Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Beaux-Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts architecture</a></div>

</ul>
<p>The Beaux-Arts style takes its name from the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_des_Beaux-Arts" title="École des Beaux-Arts">École des Beaux-Arts</a> in Paris, where it developed and where many of the main exponents of the style studied. Due to the fact that international students studied here, there are buildings from the second half of the 19th century and the early 20th century of this type all over the world, designed by architects like <a href="/wiki/Charles_Girault" title="Charles Girault">Charles Girault</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hastings_(architect)" title="Thomas Hastings (architect)">Thomas Hastings</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ion_D._Berindey&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ion D. Berindey (page does not exist)">Ion D. Berindey</a> or <a href="/wiki/Petre_Antonescu" title="Petre Antonescu">Petre Antonescu</a>. Today, from <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a> to <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> and from <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> to <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, the Beaux-Arts style survives in opera houses, civic structures, university campuses commemorative monuments, luxury hotels and townhouses. The style was heavily influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Palais_Garnier" title="Palais Garnier">Paris Opéra House</a> (1860-1875), designed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Garnier_(architect)" title="Charles Garnier (architect)">Charles Garnier</a>, the masterpiece of the 19th century <a href="/wiki/Haussmann%27s_renovation_of_Paris" title="Haussmann's renovation of Paris">renovation of Paris</a>, dominating its entire neighbourhood and continuing to astonish visitors with its majestic staircase and reception halls. The Opéra was an aesthetic and societal turning point in French architecture. Here, Garnier showed what he called a <i>style actuel</i>, which was influenced by the spirit of the time, aka <a href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">Zeitgeist</a>, and reflected the designer's personal taste.
</p><p>Beaux-Arts façades were usually imbricated, or layered with overlapping classical elements or sculpture. Often façades consisted of a high rusticated basement level, after it a few floors high level, usually decorated with pilasters or columns, and at the top an attic level and/or the roof. Beaux-Arts architects were often commissioned to design monumental civic buildings symbolic of the self-confidence of the town or city. The style aimed for a <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a> opulence through lavishly decorated monumental structures that evoked <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Versailles" title="Versailles">Versailles</a>. However, it wasn't just a revival of the Baroque, being more of a synthesis of Classicist styles, like <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance</a>, Baroque, <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassicism</a> etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014292,_295,_296_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014292,_295,_296-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHopkins2014132,_133,_134,_135_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHopkins2014132,_133,_134,_135-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey2012413_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey2012413-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_and_new_technologies">Industry and new technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Industry and new technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 220.66666666667px"><div style="width: 220.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 218.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antwerpen,_centraal_station_oeg7051_vanaf_perron4_IMG_1134_2017-08-27_14.01.jpg" title="Antwerpen-Centraal railway station, 1895–1905, by Louis Delacenserie[198]"><img alt="Antwerpen-Centraal railway station, 1895–1905, by Louis Delacenserie[198]" data-file-height="2823" data-file-width="4116" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Antwerpen%2C_centraal_station_oeg7051_vanaf_perron4_IMG_1134_2017-08-27_14.01.jpg/328px-Antwerpen%2C_centraal_station_oeg7051_vanaf_perron4_IMG_1134_2017-08-27_14.01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Antwerpen%2C_centraal_station_oeg7051_vanaf_perron4_IMG_1134_2017-08-27_14.01.jpg/493px-Antwerpen%2C_centraal_station_oeg7051_vanaf_perron4_IMG_1134_2017-08-27_14.01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Antwerpen%2C_centraal_station_oeg7051_vanaf_perron4_IMG_1134_2017-08-27_14.01.jpg/656px-Antwerpen%2C_centraal_station_oeg7051_vanaf_perron4_IMG_1134_2017-08-27_14.01.jpg 2x" width="219"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Antwerpen-Centraal_railway_station" title="Antwerpen-Centraal railway station">Antwerpen-Centraal railway station</a>, 1895–1905, by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Delacenserie" title="Louis Delacenserie">Louis Delacenserie</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrving2019339_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrving2019339-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Because of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> and the new technologies it brought, new types of buildings have appeared. By 1850 iron was quite present in dailylife at every scale, from mass-produced decorative architectural details and objects of apartment buildings and commercial buildings to train sheds. A well-known 19th century glass and iron building is <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">the Crystal Palace</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park</a> (London), built in 1851 to house the <a href="/wiki/Great_Exhibition" title="Great Exhibition">Great Exhibition</a>, having an appearance similar with a greenhouse. Its scale was daunting.
</p><p>The marketplace pioneered novel uses of iron and glass to create an architecture of display and consumption that made the temporary display of the world fairs a permanent feature of modern urban life. Just after a year after the Crystal Palace was dismantaled, <a href="/wiki/Aristide_Boucicaut" title="Aristide Boucicaut">Aristide Boucicaut</a> opened what historians of mass consumption have labelled the first <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department store</a>, <a href="/wiki/Le_Bon_March%C3%A9" title="Le Bon Marché">Le Bon Marché</a> in Paris. As the store expanded, its exterior took on the form of a public monument, being highly decorated with French <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Revival_architecture" title="Renaissance Revival architecture">Renaissance Revival</a> motifs. The entrances advanced subtly onto the pavemenet, hoping to captivate the attention of potential customers. Between 1872 and 1874, the interior was remodelled by <a href="/wiki/Louis-Charles_Boileau" title="Louis-Charles Boileau">Louis-Charles Boileau</a>, in collaboration with the young engineering firm of <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Eiffel" title="Gustave Eiffel">Gustave Eiffel</a>. In place of the open courtyard required to permit more daylight into the interior, the new building focused around three skylight atria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000207,_237,_238_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000207,_237,_238-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_Nouveau">Art Nouveau</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Art Nouveau">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau#Architecture_and_ornamentation" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau § Architecture and ornamentation</a></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Popular in many countries from the early 1890s until the outbreak of World War I in 1914, Art Nouveau was an influential although relatively brief art and design movement and philosophy. Despite being a short-lived fashion, it paved the way for the <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modern architecture</a> of the 20th century. Between <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1870 and 1900, a crisis of historicism occurred, during which the historicist culture was critiqued, one of the voices being <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> in 1874, who diagnosed 'a malignant historical fervour' as one of the crippling symptoms of a modern culture burdened by archaeological study and faith in the laws of historical progression.
</p>

class="tsingle" style="width:203px;max-width:203px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:254px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Entr%C3%A9e_dun_immeuble_art_nouveau_(Paris)_(4807519051).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="946" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Entr%C3%A9e_dun_immeuble_art_nouveau_%28Paris%29_%284807519051%29.jpg/201px-Entr%C3%A9e_dun_immeuble_art_nouveau_%28Paris%29_%284807519051%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Entr%C3%A9e_dun_immeuble_art_nouveau_%28Paris%29_%284807519051%29.jpg/302px-Entr%C3%A9e_dun_immeuble_art_nouveau_%28Paris%29_%284807519051%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Entr%C3%A9e_dun_immeuble_art_nouveau_%28Paris%29_%284807519051%29.jpg/402px-Entr%C3%A9e_dun_immeuble_art_nouveau_%28Paris%29_%284807519051%29.jpg 2x" width="201"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Les Chardons Building (Paris), 1903, by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Klein" title="Charles Klein">Charles Klein</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Focusing on natural forms, asymmetry, sinuous lines and whiplash curves, architects and designers aimed to escape the excessively ornamental styles and historical replications, popular during the 19th century. However, the style wasn't completely new, since Art Nouveau artists drew on a huge range of influences, particularly <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts architecture</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arts_and_Crafts_movement" title="Arts and Crafts movement">Arts and Crafts movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aestheticism" title="Aestheticism">aestheticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_art" title="Japanese art">Japanese</a> art. Buildings used materials associated in the 19th century with modernity, such as cast-iron and glass. A good example of this is the Paris Metro entrance at <a href="/wiki/Porte_Dauphine_(Paris_M%C3%A9tro)" title="Porte Dauphine (Paris Métro)">Porte Dauphine</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hector_Guimard" title="Hector Guimard">Hector Guimard</a> (1900). Its cast-iron and glass canopy is as much sculpture as it is architecture. In Paris, Art Nouveau was even called Le Style Métro by some. The interest for stylized organic forms of ornamentation originated in the mid 19th century, when it was promoted in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Grammar_of_Ornament" title="The Grammar of Ornament">The Grammar of Ornament</a></i> (1854), a pattern book by British architect <a href="/wiki/Owen_Jones_(architect)" title="Owen Jones (architect)">Owen Jones (architect)</a> (1809-1874).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Whiplash_(decorative_art)" title="Whiplash (decorative art)">Whiplash</a> curves and sinuous organic lines are its most familiar hallmarks, however the style can not be summarized only to them, since its forms are much more varied and complex. The movement displayed many national interpretations. Depending on where it manifested, it was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Celtic_art" title="Celtic art">Celtic art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic Revival</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rococo_Revival" title="Rococo Revival">Rococo Revival</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baroque_Revival_architecture" title="Baroque Revival architecture">Baroque Revival</a>. In Hungary, Romania and Poland, for example, Art Nouveau incorporated folkloric elements. This is true especially in Romania, because it facilitated the appearance of the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_Revival_architecture" title="Romanian Revival architecture">Romanian Revival</a> style, which draws inspiration from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%A2ncovenesc_architecture" title="Brâncovenesc architecture">Brâncovenesc architecture</a> and traditional peasant houses and objects. The style also had different names, depending on countries. In Britain it was known as <i>Modern Style</i>, in the Netherlands as <i>Nieuwe Kunst</i>, in Germany and Austria as <i>Jugendstil</i>, in Italy as <i><a href="/wiki/Liberty_style" title="Liberty style">Liberty style</a></i>, in Romania as <i>Arta 1900</i>, and in Japan as <i>Shiro-Uma</i>. It would be wrong to credit any particular place as the only one where the movement appeared, since it seems to have arisen in multiple locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000269,_279_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergdoll2000269,_279-211">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014320,_321,_322_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014320,_321,_322-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201936_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201936-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Rejecting ornament and embracing minimalism and modern materials, Modernist architecture appeared across the world from the early 20th century. <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a> paved the way for it, promoting the idea of non-historicist styles. It developed initially in Europe, focusing on functionalism and the avoidance of decoration. Modernism reached its peak during the 1920s and 1930s with the <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Style_(architecture)" title="International Style (architecture)">International Style</a>, both characterised by asymmetry, flat roofs, large windows, metal, glass, white rendering and open-plan interiors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201937_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201937-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb" style="width: 225.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biarritz_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_mer.jpg" title="Musée de la Mer (Biarritz, France), 1933, by Joseph Hiriart[222]"><img alt="Musée de la Mer (Biarritz, France), 1933, by Joseph Hiriart[222]" data-file-height="3514" data-file-width="5271" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Biarritz_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_mer.jpg/338px-Biarritz_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_mer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Biarritz_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_mer.jpg/507px-Biarritz_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_mer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Biarritz_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_mer.jpg/675px-Biarritz_-_Mus%C3%A9e_de_la_mer.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div>

<p><a href="/wiki/Biarritz_Aquarium" title="Biarritz Aquarium">Musée de la Mer</a> (<a href="/wiki/Biarritz" title="Biarritz">Biarritz</a>, France), 1933, by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hiriart" title="Joseph Hiriart">Joseph Hiriart</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014360_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014360-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 267.33333333333px"><div style="width: 267.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 265.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radio_Kootwijk_Zendgebouw.jpg" title="Radio Kootwijk in Kootwijk, The Netherlands (1927), by Julius Luthmann"><img alt="Radio Kootwijk in Kootwijk, The Netherlands (1927), by Julius Luthmann" data-file-height="1688" data-file-width="2986" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Radio_Kootwijk_Zendgebouw.jpg/398px-Radio_Kootwijk_Zendgebouw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Radio_Kootwijk_Zendgebouw.jpg/598px-Radio_Kootwijk_Zendgebouw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Radio_Kootwijk_Zendgebouw.jpg/796px-Radio_Kootwijk_Zendgebouw.jpg 2x" width="266"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Radio_Kootwijk" title="Radio Kootwijk">Radio Kootwijk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kootwijk" title="Kootwijk">Kootwijk</a>, The Netherlands (1927), by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Julius_Luthmann&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Julius Luthmann (page does not exist)">Julius Luthmann</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Art Deco, named retrospectively after an exhibition held in Paris in 1925, originated in France as a luxurious, highly decorated style. It then spread quickly throughout the world - most dramatically in the United States - becoming more <a href="/wiki/Streamline_Moderne" title="Streamline Moderne">streamlined</a> and modernistic through the 1930s. The style was pervasive and popular, finding its way into the design of everything from jewellery to film sets, from the interiors of ordinary homes to cinemas, luxury streamliners and hotels. Its exuberance and fantasy captured the spirit of the 'roaring 20s' and provided an escape from the realities of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> during the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although it ended with the start of World War II, its appeal has endured. Despite that it is an example of modern architecture, elements of the style drew on <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_architecture" title="Ancient Egyptian architecture">ancient Egyptian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_architecture" title="Ancient Greek architecture">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_architecture" title="Ancient Roman architecture">Roman</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_architecture" title="African architecture">African</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztec</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese</a> influences, but also on <a href="/wiki/Futurism" title="Futurism">Futurism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a>. Bold colours were often applied on low-reliefs. Predominant materials include <a href="/wiki/Chrome_plating" title="Chrome plating">chrome plating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a>, polished <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, inlaid wood, stone and stained glass.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Style">International Style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: International Style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_Style_(architecture)" title="International Style (architecture)">International Style (architecture)</a></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The International Style emerged in Europe after World War I, influenced by recent movements, including <a href="/wiki/De_Stijl" title="De Stijl">De Stijl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Streamline_Moderne" title="Streamline Moderne">Streamline Moderne</a>, and had a close relationship to the <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a>. The antithesis of nearly every other architectural movement that preceded it, the International Style eliminated extraneous ornament and used modern industrial materials such as steel, glass, reinforced concrete and <a href="/wiki/Chrome_plating" title="Chrome plating">chrome plating</a>. Rectilinear, flat-roofed, asymmetrical and white, it became a symbol of modernity across the world. It seemed to offer a crisp, clean, rational future after the horrors of war. Named by the architect <a href="/wiki/Philip_Johnson" title="Philip Johnson">Philip Johnson</a> and historian <a href="/wiki/Henry-Russell_Hitchcock" title="Henry-Russell Hitchcock">Henry-Russell Hitchcock</a> (1903-1987) in 1932, the movement was epitomized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles-Edouard_Jeanneret" title="Charles-Edouard Jeanneret">Charles-Edouard Jeanneret</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Le_Corbusier" title="Le Corbusier">Le Corbusier</a> and was clearly expressed in his statement that 'a house is a machine for living in'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201942_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201942-229">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brutalist">Brutalist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Brutalist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Brutalist_architecture" title="Brutalist architecture">Brutalist architecture</a></div>

<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Freeway_Park" title="Freeway Park">Freeway Park</a> Fountain (<a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, USA), 1976, by <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Halprin" title="Lawrence Halprin">Lawrence Halprin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2014428_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2014428-234">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Based on social equality, Brutalism was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Le_Corbusier" title="Le Corbusier">Le Corbusier</a>'s 1947-1952 <a href="/wiki/Unit%C3%A9_d%27habitation" title="Unité d'habitation">Unité d'habitation</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marseilles" title="Marseilles">Marseilles</a>. It seems the term was originally coined by Swedish architect <a href="/wiki/Hans_Asplund" title="Hans Asplund">Hans Asplund</a> (1921-1994), but Le Corbusier's use of the description <i>béton brut</i>, meaning raw concrete, for his choice of material for the Unité d'habitation was particularly influential. The style flourished from the 1950s to the mid-1970s, mainly using concrete, which although new in itself, was unconventional when exposed on facades. Before Brutalism, concrete was usually hidden beneath other materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201946_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201946-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postmodern">Postmodern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_architecture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Postmodern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">Postmodern architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 258px"><div style="width: 258px">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>No one definable style, Postmodernism is an eclectic mix of approaches that appeared in the late 20th century in reaction against Modernism, which was increasingly perceived as monotonous and conservative. As with many movements, a complete antithesis to Modernism developed. In 1966, the architect <a href="/wiki/Robert_Venturi" title="Robert Venturi">Robert Venturi</a> (1925-2018) had published his book, <i>Complexity and Contradiction in Architecture</i>, which praised the originality and creativity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mannerist_architecture" title="Mannerist architecture">Mannerist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque architecture</a> of Rome, and encouraged more ambiguity and complexity in contemporary design. Complaining about the austerity and tedium of so many smooth steel and glass Modernist buildings, and in deliberate denunciation of the famous Modernist 'Less is more', Venturi stated 'Less is a bore'. His theories became a majore influence on the development of Postmodernism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge201947_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge201947-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p>

</ul>
<p>Deconstructivism in architecture is a development of <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">postmodern architecture</a> that began in the late 1980s. It is characterized by ideas of fragmentation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonlinear_(arts)" title="Nonlinear (arts)">non-linear</a> processes of design, an interest in manipulating ideas of a structure's surface or skin, and apparent <a href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry" title="Non-Euclidean geometry">non-Euclidean geometry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> (i.e., non-<a href="/wiki/Rectilinear_polygon" title="Rectilinear polygon">rectilinear</a> shapes) which serve to distort and dislocate some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Design_principles_and_elements" title="Design principles and elements">elements of architecture</a>, such as structure and <a href="/wiki/Building_envelope" title="Building envelope">envelope</a>. The finished visual appearance of buildings that exhibit the many deconstructivist "styles" is characterised by a stimulating unpredictability and a controlled chaos.
</p><p>Important events in the history of the Deconstructivist movement include the 1982 <a href="/wiki/Parc_de_la_Villette" title="Parc de la Villette">Parc de la Villette</a> <a href="/wiki/Architectural_design_competition" title="Architectural design competition">architectural design competition</a> (especially the entry from the French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida">Jacques Derrida</a> and the American architect <a href="/wiki/Peter_Eisenman" title="Peter Eisenman">Peter Eisenman</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Tschumi" title="Bernard Tschumi">Bernard Tschumi</a>'s winning entry), the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a>'s 1988 <i>Deconstructivist Architecture</i> exhibition in New York, organized by <a href="/wiki/Philip_Johnson" title="Philip Johnson">Philip Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Wigley" title="Mark Wigley">Mark Wigley</a>, and the 1989 opening of the <a href="/wiki/Wexner_Center_for_the_Arts" title="Wexner Center for the Arts">Wexner Center for the Arts</a> in <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus</a>, designed by Peter Eisenman. The New York exhibition featured works by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Gehry" title="Frank Gehry">Frank Gehry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Libeskind" title="Daniel Libeskind">Daniel Libeskind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rem_Koolhaas" title="Rem Koolhaas">Rem Koolhaas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Eisenman" title="Peter Eisenman">Peter Eisenman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaha_Hadid" title="Zaha Hadid">Zaha Hadid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coop_Himmelblau" title="Coop Himmelblau">Coop Himmelblau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Tschumi" title="Bernard Tschumi">Bernard Tschumi</a>. Since the exhibition, many of the architects who were associated with Deconstructivism have distanced themselves from the term. Nonetheless, the term has stuck and has now, in fact, come to embrace a general trend within contemporary architecture.
</p>

<ul>
